Rational Judgments
intermediate
6 min
Second Proof: Why Human Moral Categories Don't Apply to God's Actions
The author proves by conditional reasoning that applying human moral judgment to Allah leads to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that the intellect's moral judgment (تحسين/تقبيح) cannot be applied to Allah's actions, specifically that applying it would lead to absurd consequences regarding creation.

Core Idea
If human reason could judge God's actions, creation would be evil — and that is impossible.

The author presents a powerful proof by conditional reasoning: if we accept that our intellect can morally approve or disapprove of God's actions, then we must judge creation itself as evil. Since that is clearly false, the premise is false. This lesson walks through the logical steps that expose the absurdity, showing why creation cannot be for God's own benefit.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional premise
Start by grasping the central conditional: if intellect's approval/disapproval is valid in divine matters, then creation would be deemed 'evil' by that standard. Since creation obviously is not evil, the antecedent must be false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
2
Explore the two possibilities for creation
The author breaks down the conditional: events (creation) are either for a benefit or not. If not for benefit, they are futile and thus evil. So they must be for a benefit.
3
Trace the benefit: to Creator or creature?
That benefit must return either to Allah or to the creature. The passage then attacks the possibility of benefit to Allah, showing two absurd consequences.
4
Examine the two absurdities for benefit to Allah
First, benefit to Allah implies He is in need, which is impossible. Second, that need must be either eternal or originated; both lead to contradictions (eternal need => eternal creation; originated need => infinite regress).
Focus on understanding why each leads to impossibility.
5
Conclude the invalidity of the premise
Since both branches lead to absurdity, the initial conditional premise is false. Therefore, the intellect's moral judgment does not apply to Allah's actions.
This proof reinforces the transcendence of Allah beyond human moral categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: لو كان تقبيح العقل وتحسينه معتبراً في أفعال الله وفي أحكامه لزم أن يقبح منه الخلق. وذلك باطل. فهذا باطل. بيان الشرطية: أن الأحداث إما أن يكون لنفع ومصلحة، أولاً لمصلحة.. والأول باطل. لأن ذلك النفع إما أن يكون عائداً إلى الخالق أو إلى المخلوق والأول: باطل لوجهين: الأول: إن ذلك يوجب كونه تعالى محتاجاً وهو محال. والثاني: إن تلك الحجة إما أن تكون قديمة أو حادثة، فإن كانت قديمة وجب أن يحصل تخليق العالم قبل ما وقع، لأن المقتضى للتخليق، لما كان هو تلك الحاجة وكانت تلك الحاجة حاصلة قبل ذلك الوقت، فحينئذ يكون المقتضى لحدوث ذلك الحادث حاصلاً قبل حدوث ذلك الحادث، سليماً عن المعارض. فوجب أن يحدث ذلك الحادث قبل أن يحدث وذلك محال. وإما إن كانت الحاجة حادثة عاد الكلام في المقتضى لحدوث الحاجة، ولزم التسلسل.
The second proof: if the intellect's disapproval and approval were considered in Allah's actions and His rulings, it would necessitate that creation (خلق) be evil by Him. That is false. So this is false. Explanation of the conditional: that events (أحداث) are either for benefit and interest (نفع ومصلحة) or not for interest. The first is false. Because that benefit is either returning to the Creator or to the creature. The first is false for two reasons: the first: that necessitates that He, exalted, be in need, which is impossible. The second: that that need is either ancient or originated. If ancient, then it would be necessary that the creation of the world happen before it occurred, because the cause (مقتضى) for creation, since it is that need and that need was present before that time, then the cause for the occurrence of that event would be present before the occurrence of that event, unsullied by opposition. So it would be necessary that the event happen before it happened, which is impossible. And if the need is originated, the speech returns to the cause for the origin of the need, and an infinite regress (تسلسل) follows.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof that the intellect's moral judgment (تحسين وتقبيح) does not apply to God. The conditional premise: if it did apply, then creation (خلق) would be judged evil by the intellect, which is false. To explain the conditional, the author argues that all events (creation) must be for some benefit or interest. If not, they would be futile, which reason would deem evil. So events are for a benefit. That benefit must either return to God or to the creature. If to God, it implies God is in need, which is impossible. Furthermore, if that need is eternal, then creation would have occurred eternally (since the cause would be present eternally), which is false. If the need is originated, then we must ask what caused that need, leading to an infinite regress. Thus, the benefit cannot be for God. Therefore, the benefit is for the creature. But then the argument proceeds (not fully given in this window) to show further absurdities. The conclusion is that the intellectual standard of good and evil cannot be applied to God's actions.

Key Terms
تقبيح
تحسين
خلق
أحداث
نفع ومصلحة
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is denying that God's actions have any purpose.
Correction: The proof shows that applying human moral categories to divine actions leads to absurdity, not that God's actions lack purpose. Purpose is affirmed but beyond human moral evaluation.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, if creation were for the benefit of the Creator (الله), what problem arises?
A) It would imply God is unjust
B) It would imply God is in need, which is impossible
C) It would make creation meaningless
D) It would require infinite benefits

Key Takeaway
This proof demonstrates that human moral categories are inapplicable to divine actions because they lead to logical absurdities such as implying God's neediness or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a trial or a situation you perceive as unjust, do you find your heart questioning Allah's wisdom? How does this proof challenge that instinct? What would it look like for you to actively surrender that judgment to Allah this week, especially in something you have been struggling to accept?
?
The proof shows that if we apply human standards of good and evil to Allah, we end up in absurdities. Consider a specific command or prohibition in Islam that your intellect finds difficult or unreasonable. In light of this lesson, how can you train yourself to silence that inner critic and submit fully to Allah's decree, even when you do not see the benefit?
?
This lesson argues that our reason cannot judge Allah's actions because it leads to contradictions. Is there an area of your life where you are still using your own sense of 'what should be' instead of trusting Allah's plan? Write down one concrete decision you will make today to hand that judgment over to Allah.

Rational Judgments

5 min
The Conditional Core of the Second Argument
Al-Razi states the conditional and introduces the purpose analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of al-Razi's conditional proof: if rational judgment applied to God, creation would be deemed evil; since creation is not evil, rational judgment does not apply.

Core Idea
If rational judgment applied, creation would be evil – but it isn't, so it doesn't.

Al-Razi opens his second argument with a clear conditional. He states that if rational judgments of good and evil were valid for God, then creation would necessarily be deemed evil from Him. Since that conclusion is false, the premise must be false. The rest of the passage unpacks this conditional by examining every possible reason why God might create.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening conditional
Start with the first sentence: 'If rational judgment were considered valid regarding God's actions, then creation would be deemed evil from Him.' Notice this sets up a logical implication.
This is a classic qiyas method: show that the premise leads to an absurdity.
2
Understand the refutation pattern
Al-Razi declares the conditional false because its consequent is false. But he then explains why the conditional holds—that is, why if the premise were true, creation would be evil. He does this by analyzing all possible purposes for creation.
The argument is not merely asserting; it proves the conditional by exhaustive analysis.
3
Prepare for the divisions
The author divides the purpose of creation into three categories. This lesson covers only the overall structure; subsequent lessons explore each category in detail.
This sets the stage for a detailed case-by-case refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : لو كان تقبيح العقل وتحسينه معتبراً في أفعال الله وفي أحكامه لزم أن يقبح منه الخلق . وذلك باطل . فهذا باطل . بيان الشرطية : أن الأحداث إما أن يكون لنفع ومصلحة ، أولاً لمصلحة .. والأول باطل . لأن ذلك النفع إما أن يكون عائداً إلى الخالق أو إلى المخلوق والأول : باطل لوجهين : الأول : إن ذلك يوجب كونه تعالى محتاجاً وهو محال . والثاني : إن تلك الحجة إما أن تكون قديمة أو حادثة ، فإن كانت قديمة وجب أن يحصل تخليق العالم قبل ما وقع ، لأن المقتضى للتخليق ، لما كان هو تلك الحاجة وكانت تلك الحاجة حاصلة قبل ذلك الوقت ، فحينئذ يكون المقتضى لحدوث ذلك الحادث حاصلاً قبل حدوث ذلك الحادث ، سليماً عن المعارض . فوجب أن يحدث ذلك الحادث قبل أن يحدث وذلك محال . وإما إن كانت الحاجة حادثة عاد الكلام في المقتضى لحدوث الحاجة ، ولزم التسلسل . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إنه تعالى إنما أحدث العالم لنفع عائد إلى المخلوق . فهذا أيضاً : باطل . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنه ما كان في العدم شيء يحتاج إلى شيء حتى يكون الإيجاد إحساناً إليه ، بل نقول : إن ذلك يوجب الدور ، لأنه لا يصير المخلوق موجوداً إلا بإيجاد الخالق ، ولا يحصل إيجاد الخالق إلا إذا كان إحساناً ، ولا يكون إحساناً إلا إذا كان ذلك المخلوق موجوداً ، حتى يكون ذلك الإيجاد إحساناً إليه ، فيثبت أن هذا يوجب الدور . وأنه محال والثاني : إن الإحسان لا يكون إحساناً إلا إذا كان مسبوقاً بحصول الحاجة والشهوة فيتقابل هذا النفع بذلك الضرر السابق ، وذلك يقدح في كونه إحساناً . وقد سبق تقرير هذا الكلام بالاستقصاء : والثالث : وهو أن ذلك الإحسان إما أن يحصل في الدنيا ، أو في الآخرة . أما في الدنيا فباطل . لأنها دار البلاء والشقاء والغموم والهموم ، وحصول الأخلاق (1) المؤذية كالحرص والحاجة . وأما في الآخرة فالأكثرون هم الكفار وهم (٢) أهل العذاب الدائم وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : إنه تعالى خلق الخلق لا لنفع أصلاً : فهذا عبث ، وهو قبيح في العقول فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن حكم العقل في التحسين والتقبيح لو كان معتبراً في أفعال الله وفي أحكامه لقبح من الله الخلق ، وحيث لم يكن كذلك ، علمنا أنه باطل . والله أعلم
The second proof: If rational judgment of good and evil were considered valid regarding God's actions and His rulings, then creation would necessarily be deemed evil from Him. And that is false. So this [conditional] is false. Explanation of the conditional: Events are either for benefit and advantage, or not for advantage. The first is false. Because that benefit either returns to the Creator or to the creature. The first is false for two reasons: First, that entails that He, exalted, is in need, which is impossible. Second, that need is either eternal or originated. If it were eternal, then the creation of the world would have to occur before it occurred, because the motive for creation, being that need, and that need being present before that time, then the motive for the occurrence of that event would be present before the occurrence of that event, free of contrary. So that event would have to occur before it occurred, which is impossible. And if the need were originated, the discussion returns to the motive for the occurrence of the need, leading to an infinite regress. As for the second division: that He, exalted, created the world for a benefit returning to the creature. This too is false. Evidence for it are aspects: First, there was nothing in non-existence that needed anything such that existence would be a favor to it. Rather, we say: that entails circularity, because the creature does not become existent except by the Creator's bringing into existence, and the Creator's bringing into existence does not occur unless it is a favor, and it is not a favor unless that creature exists, so that that bringing into existence is a favor to it. Thus it is established that this entails circularity, which is impossible. Second, a favor is not a favor unless it is preceded by the occurrence of need and desire, so this benefit is counterbalanced by that prior harm, and that impairs its being a favor. And this discourse has already been exhaustively presented. Third, that favor either occurs in this world or the hereafter. As for this world, it is false because it is the abode of affliction, misery, griefs, and worries, and the occurrence of harmful traits such as greed and need. And as for the hereafter, the majority are the disbelievers, and they are the people of perpetual punishment. As for the third division: that He, exalted, created creation for no benefit at all: this is pointless, and it is deemed evil by intellects. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: that if rational judgment of good and evil were considered valid regarding God's actions and His rulings, then creation would necessarily be deemed evil from God. And since it is not so, we know that it is false. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-ḥujja al-thāniya) against the validity of rational judgment of good and evil (al-taḥsīn wa al-taqbīḥ al-ʿaqliyy) in God's actions and rulings. The argument is structured as a conditional proof: if rational judgment applied to God, then creation would be deemed evil (qabīḥ) from Him. Since creation is not evil, rational judgment does not apply. The author then explains the conditional by analyzing the purpose of creation. He posits three possibilities for why God creates: (1) for a benefit accruing to the Creator, (2) for a benefit accruing to the creature, or (3) for no benefit at all. Each is refuted. The first possibility is false because it implies need in God, which is impossible, and leads to either a temporal priority problem or infinite regress. The second possibility is false for three reasons: circularity (dawr), because the creature must exist for the favor to be a favor; the favor is counterbalanced by prior harm; and the benefits of this world and the hereafter do not genuinely favor most people. The third possibility is false because it would be pointless (ʿabath), which is deemed evil by intellects. Since all possibilities are false, the conditional holds: if rational judgment applied, creation would be evil. But creation is not evil, so rational judgment does not apply to God. The argument concludes that rational judgment is invalid in the divine context.

Key Terms
التحسين والتقبيح العقلي
الحجة الثانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the argument simply assumes rational judgment does not apply to God without proof.
Correction: Al-Razi does not assume; he provides a rigorous conditional proof that shows accepting rational judgment leads to an absurd conclusion, thereby proving its invalidity.

Checkpoint
What is the main conditional stated at the beginning of the second argument?
A) If creation is evil, then rational judgment applies to God.
B) If rational judgment applies to God, then creation is evil.
C) If creation is not evil, then rational judgment does not apply.
D) If God creates for benefit, then creation is good.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's second argument is a conditional proof: accepting rational judgment about God leads to the absurd conclusion that creation is evil, so the premise must be rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God is beyond human moral categories, how does that change the way you judge events in your own life that seem unfair or evil? Are you willing to stop applying your own standards of good and evil to God's actions?
?
Al-Razi says creation would be deemed evil if human reason were the judge. When you look at the world, do you ever find yourself silently judging God's actions as 'wrong' or 'unjust'? What does this passage demand you do with that feeling?
?
You are not God's moral critic. If you accept that, what must change about how you process your deepest disappointments with life? Make one concrete commitment to stop measuring God by your scale of justice.

Rational Judgments
advanced
8 min
Refuting Benefit to the Creator
Al-Razi refutes the first possibility: creation for Creator's benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why creation cannot be for a benefit returning to the Creator, due to the impossibility of divine need and the logical problems of temporal priority and infinite regress.

Core Idea
A benefit for God implies need in God, which is impossible.

Having divided the possibilities, al-Razi first examines the case where creation is for the Creator's own benefit. He argues this is impossible because it would entail need (ḥāja) in God, which is a deficiency. Moreover, even if we consider that need as eternal or originated, both lead to logical contradictions: either the world would have to exist before it existed, or an infinite regress of needs occurs.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of benefit to Creator
Read the Arabic: 'لأن ذلك النفع إما أن يكون عائداً إلى الخالق أو إلى المخلوق والأول: باطل لوجهين...' Focus on the two reasons given.
The first reason is that benefit implies need; the second dissects the nature of that need.
2
Understand the first reason: need in God
Al-Razi says benefit to Creator necessitates that He is in need (ḥāja), which is impossible for the perfect Creator.
This is a foundational theological principle: God is self-sufficient.
3
Analyze the second reason: temporal paradox
If the need is eternal, the world should have been created earlier (since motive always present) – a contradiction. If the need is originated, then we need a cause for that need, leading to infinite regress.
This is a classic kalam argument using the impossibility of infinite regress (tasalsul).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : لو كان تقبيح العقل وتحسينه معتبراً في أفعال الله وفي أحكامه لزم أن يقبح منه الخلق . وذلك باطل . فهذا باطل . بيان الشرطية : أن الأحداث إما أن يكون لنفع ومصلحة ، أولاً لمصلحة .. والأول باطل . لأن ذلك النفع إما أن يكون عائداً إلى الخالق أو إلى المخلوق والأول : باطل لوجهين : الأول : إن ذلك يوجب كونه تعالى محتاجاً وهو محال . والثاني : إن تلك الحجة إما أن تكون قديمة أو حادثة ، فإن كانت قديمة وجب أن يحصل تخليق العالم قبل ما وقع ، لأن المقتضى للتخليق ، لما كان هو تلك الحاجة وكانت تلك الحاجة حاصلة قبل ذلك الوقت ، فحينئذ يكون المقتضى لحدوث ذلك الحادث حاصلاً قبل حدوث ذلك الحادث ، سليماً عن المعارض . فوجب أن يحدث ذلك الحادث قبل أن يحدث وذلك محال . وإما إن كانت الحاجة حادثة عاد الكلام في المقتضى لحدوث الحاجة ، ولزم التسلسل . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إنه تعالى إنما أحدث العالم لنفع عائد إلى المخلوق . فهذا أيضاً : باطل . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنه ما كان في العدم شيء يحتاج إلى شيء حتى يكون الإيجاد إحساناً إليه ، بل نقول : إن ذلك يوجب الدور ، لأنه لا يصير المخلوق موجوداً إلا بإيجاد الخالق ، ولا يحصل إيجاد الخالق إلا إذا كان إحساناً ، ولا يكون إحساناً إلا إذا كان ذلك المخلوق موجوداً ، حتى يكون ذلك الإيجاد إحساناً إليه ، فيثبت أن هذا يوجب الدور . وأنه محال والثاني : إن الإحسان لا يكون إحساناً إلا إذا كان مسبوقاً بحصول الحاجة والشهوة فيتقابل هذا النفع بذلك الضرر السابق ، وذلك يقدح في كونه إحساناً . وقد سبق تقرير هذا الكلام بالاستقصاء : والثالث : وهو أن ذلك الإحسان إما أن يحصل في الدنيا ، أو في الآخرة . أما في الدنيا فباطل . لأنها دار البلاء والشقاء والغموم والهموم ، وحصول الأخلاق (1) المؤذية كالحرص والحاجة . وأما في الآخرة فالأكثرون هم الكفار وهم (٢) أهل العذاب الدائم وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : إنه تعالى خلق الخلق لا لنفع أصلاً : فهذا عبث ، وهو قبيح في العقول فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن حكم العقل في التحسين والتقبيح لو كان معتبراً في أفعال الله وفي أحكامه لقبح من الله الخلق ، وحيث لم يكن كذلك ، علمنا أنه باطل . والله أعلم
The second proof: If rational judgment of good and evil were considered valid regarding God's actions and His rulings, then creation would necessarily be deemed evil from Him. And that is false. So this [conditional] is false. Explanation of the conditional: Events are either for benefit and advantage, or not for advantage. The first is false. Because that benefit either returns to the Creator or to the creature. The first is false for two reasons: First, that entails that He, exalted, is in need, which is impossible. Second, that need is either eternal or originated. If it were eternal, then the creation of the world would have to occur before it occurred, because the motive for creation, being that need, and that need being present before that time, then the motive for the occurrence of that event would be present before the occurrence of that event, free of contrary. So that event would have to occur before it occurred, which is impossible. And if the need were originated, the discussion returns to the motive for the occurrence of the need, leading to an infinite regress. As for the second division: that He, exalted, created the world for a benefit returning to the creature. This too is false. Evidence for it are aspects: First, there was nothing in non-existence that needed anything such that existence would be a favor to it. Rather, we say: that entails circularity, because the creature does not become existent except by the Creator's bringing into existence, and the Creator's bringing into existence does not occur unless it is a favor, and it is not a favor unless that creature exists, so that that bringing into existence is a favor to it. Thus it is established that this entails circularity, which is impossible. Second, a favor is not a favor unless it is preceded by the occurrence of need and desire, so this benefit is counterbalanced by that prior harm, and that impairs its being a favor. And this discourse has already been exhaustively presented. Third, that favor either occurs in this world or the hereafter. As for this world, it is false because it is the abode of affliction, misery, griefs, and worries, and the occurrence of harmful traits such as greed and need. And as for the hereafter, the majority are the disbelievers, and they are the people of perpetual punishment. As for the third division: that He, exalted, created creation for no benefit at all: this is pointless, and it is deemed evil by intellects. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: that if rational judgment of good and evil were considered valid regarding God's actions and His rulings, then creation would necessarily be deemed evil from God. And since it is not so, we know that it is false. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-ḥujja al-thāniya) against the validity of rational judgment of good and evil (al-taḥsīn wa al-taqbīḥ al-ʿaqliyy) in God's actions and rulings. The argument is structured as a conditional proof: if rational judgment applied to God, then creation would be deemed evil (qabīḥ) from Him. Since creation is not evil, rational judgment does not apply. The author then explains the conditional by analyzing the purpose of creation. He posits three possibilities for why God creates: (1) for a benefit accruing to the Creator, (2) for a benefit accruing to the creature, or (3) for no benefit at all. Each is refuted. The first possibility is false because it implies need in God, which is impossible, and leads to either a temporal priority problem or infinite regress. The second possibility is false for three reasons: circularity (dawr), because the creature must exist for the favor to be a favor; the favor is counterbalanced by prior harm; and the benefits of this world and the hereafter do not genuinely favor most people. The third possibility is false because it would be pointless (ʿabath), which is deemed evil by intellects. Since all possibilities are false, the conditional holds: if rational judgment applied, creation would be evil. But creation is not evil, so rational judgment does not apply to God. The argument concludes that rational judgment is invalid in the divine context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first reason given for rejecting the possibility that creation is for the Creator's benefit?
A) It leads to circularity.
B) It implies God is in need.
C) It leads to an infinite regress.
D) It makes creation pointless.

Key Takeaway
Creation cannot be for God's benefit because it would imply need in God, and any analysis of that need leads to logical absurdities (eternal world or infinite regress).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray or fast, do you ever feel that God 'needs' your worship? This passage shows that even the thought of God benefiting from your actions is a logical impossibility. How would your devotion change if you fully internalised that God is absolutely self-sufficient and your worship adds nothing to Him?
?
The second argument shows that any attempt to attribute a need to God leads to absurdities like an eternal world or infinite regress. Have you ever caught yourself thinking that God 'must' respond to your good deeds because He 'owes' you something? What specific steps can you take today to uproot that expectation and worship purely out of gratitude?
?
Al-Razi's rigorous reasoning forces us to confront our hidden assumptions about God. If He is truly free of need, then why do you still seek His approval? Is it because you fear punishment or because you love Him? Reflect on a recent act of worship: what was your primary motive? Commit to checking your intention before every prayer for the next week.

Rational Judgments

10 min
Refuting Benefit to the Creature
Al-Razi refutes the second possibility: creation for creature's benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's three arguments against creation being for the creature's benefit: circularity, the problem of prior harm, and the insufficiency of worldly and otherworldly benefits.

Core Idea
Creation cannot be a favor to creatures because it leads to circularity, harm, and mostly fails.

Al-Razi now turns to the second possibility: that God created the world for the benefit of creatures. He offers three independent refutations. The first shows a logical circularity: the creature must exist to receive the favor, but existence itself is the favor. The second argues that a favor must relieve a prior need, but the very act of creation imposes existence with its inherent harms. The third argues that the benefits of this world and the next are either absent or outweighed by suffering.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of benefit to creature
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'وأما القسم الثاني...' up to '...فإن ذلك الإحسان إما أن يحصل في الدنيا أو في الآخرة'. Focus on the three aspects (وجوه).
Al-Razi enumerates three clear reasons; identify each.
2
Understand the first reason: circularity
The favor (iḥsān) of existence requires the creature to already exist to receive it, but existence is what is being given – a vicious circle (dawr).
This is a logical argument: the effect would have to exist before its cause.
3
Understand the second reason: prior harm
A favor is meaningful only if preceded by a need or desire. But the creature before creation had no such need; moreover, existence itself brings sufferings that outweigh benefits.
Al-Razi refers to a previous detailed discussion.
4
Understand the third reason: inadequate benefits
The benefits, whether in this world (full of affliction) or the hereafter (most people are punished), do not constitute genuine favor.
The majority of humanity suffers eternally, contradicting the idea of universal favor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : لو كان تقبيح العقل وتحسينه معتبراً في أفعال الله وفي أحكامه لزم أن يقبح منه الخلق . وذلك باطل . فهذا باطل . بيان الشرطية : أن الأحداث إما أن يكون لنفع ومصلحة ، أولاً لمصلحة .. والأول باطل . لأن ذلك النفع إما أن يكون عائداً إلى الخالق أو إلى المخلوق والأول : باطل لوجهين : الأول : إن ذلك يوجب كونه تعالى محتاجاً وهو محال . والثاني : إن تلك الحجة إما أن تكون قديمة أو حادثة ، فإن كانت قديمة وجب أن يحصل تخليق العالم قبل ما وقع ، لأن المقتضى للتخليق ، لما كان هو تلك الحاجة وكانت تلك الحاجة حاصلة قبل ذلك الوقت ، فحينئذ يكون المقتضى لحدوث ذلك الحادث حاصلاً قبل حدوث ذلك الحادث ، سليماً عن المعارض . فوجب أن يحدث ذلك الحادث قبل أن يحدث وذلك محال . وإما إن كانت الحاجة حادثة عاد الكلام في المقتضى لحدوث الحاجة ، ولزم التسلسل . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إنه تعالى إنما أحدث العالم لنفع عائد إلى المخلوق . فهذا أيضاً : باطل . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنه ما كان في العدم شيء يحتاج إلى شيء حتى يكون الإيجاد إحساناً إليه ، بل نقول : إن ذلك يوجب الدور ، لأنه لا يصير المخلوق موجوداً إلا بإيجاد الخالق ، ولا يحصل إيجاد الخالق إلا إذا كان إحساناً ، ولا يكون إحساناً إلا إذا كان ذلك المخلوق موجوداً ، حتى يكون ذلك الإيجاد إحساناً إليه ، فيثبت أن هذا يوجب الدور . وأنه محال والثاني : إن الإحسان لا يكون إحساناً إلا إذا كان مسبوقاً بحصول الحاجة والشهوة فيتقابل هذا النفع بذلك الضرر السابق ، وذلك يقدح في كونه إحساناً . وقد سبق تقرير هذا الكلام بالاستقصاء : والثالث : وهو أن ذلك الإحسان إما أن يحصل في الدنيا ، أو في الآخرة . أما في الدنيا فباطل . لأنها دار البلاء والشقاء والغموم والهموم ، وحصول الأخلاق (1) المؤذية كالحرص والحاجة . وأما في الآخرة فالأكثرون هم الكفار وهم (٢) أهل العذاب الدائم وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : إنه تعالى خلق الخلق لا لنفع أصلاً : فهذا عبث ، وهو قبيح في العقول فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن حكم العقل في التحسين والتقبيح لو كان معتبراً في أفعال الله وفي أحكامه لقبح من الله الخلق ، وحيث لم يكن كذلك ، علمنا أنه باطل . والله أعلم
The second proof: If rational judgment of good and evil were considered valid regarding God's actions and His rulings, then creation would necessarily be deemed evil from Him. And that is false. So this [conditional] is false. Explanation of the conditional: Events are either for benefit and advantage, or not for advantage. The first is false. Because that benefit either returns to the Creator or to the creature. The first is false for two reasons: First, that entails that He, exalted, is in need, which is impossible. Second, that need is either eternal or originated. If it were eternal, then the creation of the world would have to occur before it occurred, because the motive for creation, being that need, and that need being present before that time, then the motive for the occurrence of that event would be present before the occurrence of that event, free of contrary. So that event would have to occur before it occurred, which is impossible. And if the need were originated, the discussion returns to the motive for the occurrence of the need, leading to an infinite regress. As for the second division: that He, exalted, created the world for a benefit returning to the creature. This too is false. Evidence for it are aspects: First, there was nothing in non-existence that needed anything such that existence would be a favor to it. Rather, we say: that entails circularity, because the creature does not become existent except by the Creator's bringing into existence, and the Creator's bringing into existence does not occur unless it is a favor, and it is not a favor unless that creature exists, so that that bringing into existence is a favor to it. Thus it is established that this entails circularity, which is impossible. Second, a favor is not a favor unless it is preceded by the occurrence of need and desire, so this benefit is counterbalanced by that prior harm, and that impairs its being a favor. And this discourse has already been exhaustively presented. Third, that favor either occurs in this world or the hereafter. As for this world, it is false because it is the abode of affliction, misery, griefs, and worries, and the occurrence of harmful traits such as greed and need. And as for the hereafter, the majority are the disbelievers, and they are the people of perpetual punishment. As for the third division: that He, exalted, created creation for no benefit at all: this is pointless, and it is deemed evil by intellects. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: that if rational judgment of good and evil were considered valid regarding God's actions and His rulings, then creation would necessarily be deemed evil from God. And since it is not so, we know that it is false. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-ḥujja al-thāniya) against the validity of rational judgment of good and evil (al-taḥsīn wa al-taqbīḥ al-ʿaqliyy) in God's actions and rulings. The argument is structured as a conditional proof: if rational judgment applied to God, then creation would be deemed evil (qabīḥ) from Him. Since creation is not evil, rational judgment does not apply. The author then explains the conditional by analyzing the purpose of creation. He posits three possibilities for why God creates: (1) for a benefit accruing to the Creator, (2) for a benefit accruing to the creature, or (3) for no benefit at all. Each is refuted. The first possibility is false because it implies need in God, which is impossible, and leads to either a temporal priority problem or infinite regress. The second possibility is false for three reasons: circularity (dawr), because the creature must exist for the favor to be a favor; the favor is counterbalanced by prior harm; and the benefits of this world and the hereafter do not genuinely favor most people. The third possibility is false because it would be pointless (ʿabath), which is deemed evil by intellects. Since all possibilities are false, the conditional holds: if rational judgment applied, creation would be evil. But creation is not evil, so rational judgment does not apply to God. The argument concludes that rational judgment is invalid in the divine context.

Key Terms
الدور
الإحسان
الضرر
الحاجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three arguments al-Razi gives against creation being for the creature's benefit?
A) Circularity
B) Infinite regress
C) Prior harm
D) Inadequate benefits in this world and the hereafter

Key Takeaway
Creation cannot be for the creature's benefit because it leads to circularity, the concept of favor is undermined by prior harm, and the actual outcomes for most creatures are not beneficial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that existence cannot be a favor because you did not need it before you were created. How does this truth affect your gratitude for being brought into existence? Do you ever feel entitled to blessings, as if God owes you happiness? Write down one specific blessing you have taken for granted and pledge to thank God for it today.
?
Al-Razi argues that this world is an abode of affliction and grief. When you face suffering, do you secretly resent God or question His justice? How can accepting that creation was never promised to be a favor free you from that resentment? Commit to one practical way to respond to hardship without complaining.
?
The passage points out that most people will be in perpetual punishment in the hereafter. This is a stark reminder that not everyone is saved. Are you living as if you are guaranteed Paradise? What specific change in your daily actions would reflect that you take this warning seriously? Name one habit you will start or stop today.

Pointlessness and Conclusion
Al-Razi refutes the third possibility and concludes the conditional proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why creation without purpose (ʿabath) is deemed evil by reason, and how this completes the conditional proof against rational judgment.

Core Idea
A purposeless act is rationally evil, so if reason judged, creation would be evil.

Al-Razi now considers the final possibility: that God created for no benefit at all. He declares this to be 'pointless' (ʿabath), which is judged evil by intellects. This completes the exhaustive analysis: all three possibilities lead to the conclusion that if rational judgment applied, creation would be evil. Since creation is not evil, rational judgment does not apply to God. The argument concludes firmly.

Guided Path
1
Read the conclusion of the argument
Read the Arabic from 'وأما القسم الثالث...' to the end. Notice how al-Razi ties the conditional together.
The final sentence restates the conditional and concludes its falsity.
2
Understand 'pointlessness' as a rational evil
Al-Razi states that creating without any benefit is 'عبث' (pointless), and that intellects deem such an act evil. This is a key premise: rational judgment includes categorizing pointless acts as evil.
This shows that even the 'no purpose' option leads to the consequent (creation evil).
3
Synthesize the entire argument
All three possibilities (benefit to Creator, to creature, or none) have been refuted as leading to absurdity or evil. Therefore the conditional holds: if rational judgment applied, creation would be evil. Since creation is not evil, rational judgment does not apply.
This is a complete reductio ad absurdum.

The Third Argument: The Promise of Punishment Would Be Evil
Al-Razi presents the third argument against rationalist ethics regarding divine threats.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's third argument against rationalist ethics, which hinges on the consensus that God's threats are not evil.

Core Idea
If rational judgment applied to God, His threats would be evil – but they are not.

In this passage, Al-Razi presents a concise conditional argument against those who claim that human reason can judge God's actions. He argues that if such rational judgment were valid, then God's promise of punishment for specific actions would be deemed evil. Yet, by universal consensus, this is not evil, so the premise must be false. The text is slightly cut off, but the logical force is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...' and the reference to 'by consensus'.
The passage ends abruptly; the conclusion is implied.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break down the argument: Premise: Rational judgment applies to God's actions. Consequence: Then it would be evil for God to threaten punishment. Consensus: This is not evil. Conclusion: Therefore, rational judgment does not apply to God's actions.
This is a modus tollens argument.
3
Reflect on 'consensus'
Consider what 'by consensus' means. It is not a human agreement but a universal rational intuition that God's threats are not evil. This is a key point to avoid misunderstanding.
The common misunderstanding is to think it refers to scholarly consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : لو كان حكم العقل بالتحسين معتبراً في أفعال الله تعالى ، وفي أفعاله لقبح من الله أن يقول للعبد : إن فعلت الفعل الفلاني أو تركت الفعل الفلاني عاقبتك . وبالاتفاق هذا لا يقبح ، فوجب أن يكون العقل
The third proof: If rational judgment of good were considered valid regarding God's actions and His deeds, then it would be deemed evil from God that He says to the servant: 'If you do such-and-such an act, or leave such-and-such an act, I will punish you.' And by consensus this is not evil, so it must be that the intellect...
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage begins the third argument (al-ḥujja al-thālitha) against rationalist ethics. The argument is presented in a conditional form: if rational judgment of good (al-taḥsīn) were valid regarding God's actions, then it would be deemed evil (qabīḥ) for God to say to a servant, 'If you do such-and-such or leave such-and-such, I will punish you.' However, by consensus (bi-al-ittifāq), this is not deemed evil. Therefore, the antecedent is false—rational judgment does not apply. The text ends abruptly with 'so it must be that the intellect (al-ʿaql)...' leaving the conclusion unstated, likely due to the page break. Based on the structure, the intended conclusion is that the intellect does not judge such threats to be evil, proving that rational judgment does not govern God's actions.

Key Terms

الاتفاق
القبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'by consensus' refers to the agreement of scholars or jurists.
Correction: In this context, 'consensus' means universal rational intuition that God's threats are not evil, not a scholarly consensus.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's third argument shows that since God's threats are not evil by consensus, rational judgment cannot apply to God's actions.

Refuting the Claim of Creation for Benefit
Three arguments against creation for creature's benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical and moral flaws in claiming that God created the world for the benefit of creatures, particularly the problem of circularity.

Core Idea
Creation cannot be for creature's benefit because benefit requires prior existence.

The author now tackles the second proposed purpose: that God created for the sake of benefiting creatures. This seems plausible, but careful analysis reveals it leads to a logical dead end. Let's examine the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage for the second division carefully, noting the three arguments provided.
2
Understand the circularity argument
Focus on the first argument: creation as beneficence requires the creature to exist to receive benefit, but existence is a result of creation, causing a loop.
This is the strongest argument.
3
Examine the second and third arguments
The second argument points out that beneficence requires prior need; the third notes that neither this world nor the Hereafter provides the intended benefit.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why these arguments invalidate the claim that God's actions are rationally evaluable as good or evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إنه تعالى إنما أحدث العالم لنفع عائد إلى المخلوق . فهذا أيضاً : باطل . ويدل عليه وجوه :
الأول : إنه ما كان في العدم شيء يحتاج إلى شيء حتى يكون الإيجاد إحساناً إليه ، بل نقول : إن ذلك يوجب الدور ، لأنه لا يصير المخلوق موجوداً إلا بإيجاد الخالق ، ولا يحصل إيجاد الخالق إلا إذا كان إحساناً ، ولا يكون إحساناً إلا إذا كان ذلك المخلوق موجوداً ، حتى يكون ذلك الإيجاد إحساناً إليه ، فيثبت أن هذا يوجب الدور . وأنه محال
والثاني : إن الإحسان لا يكون إحساناً إلا إذا كان مسبوقاً بحصول الحاجة والشهوة فيتقابل هذا النفع بذلك الضرر السابق ، وذلك يقدح في كونه إحساناً . وقد سبق تقرير هذا الكلام بالاستقصاء :
والثالث : وهو أن ذلك الإحسان إما أن يحصل في الدنيا ، أو في الآخرة . أما في الدنيا فباطل . لأنها دار البلاء والشقاء والغموم والهموم ، وحصول الأخلاف (1) المؤذية كالحرص والحاجة . وأما في الآخرة فالأكثرون هم الكفار وهم (٢) أهل العذاب الدائم
وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : إنه تعالى خلق الخلق لا لتفع أصلاً :
فهذا عبث ، وهو قبيح في العقول
فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن حكم العقل في التحسين والتقبيح لو كان معتبراً في
أفعال الله وفي أحكامه لقبح من الله الخلق ، وحيث لم يكن كذلك ، علمنا أنه
باطل . والله أعلم
As for the second division: that it is said: He, exalted, only brought the world into existence for a benefit returning to the creature. This is also false. Evidences indicate it: First: There was nothing in non-existence that needed anything so that bringing into existence would be an act of beneficence toward it. Rather we say: That entails circularity, because the creature does not become existent except by the Creator's bringing into existence, and the Creator's bringing into existence does not occur unless it is an act of beneficence, and it is not an act of beneficence unless that creature exists, so that that bringing into existence is beneficence toward it. Thus it is established that this entails circularity and that it is impossible. Second: Beneficence is not beneficence unless it is preceded by the occurrence of need and desire, so that this benefit is counterbalanced by that prior harm, and that impugns its being beneficence. And the elaboration of this statement has preceded in detail. Third: That beneficence occurs either in this world or in the Hereafter. As for this world, it is false, because it is the abode of tribulation, misery, griefs, and sorrows, and the occurrence of harmful traits like greed and need. As for the Hereafter, the majority are the disbelievers and they are the people of eternal punishment. As for the third division: that it is said: He, exalted, created creation for no purpose at all: This is absurd, and it is evil according to reason. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that if the ruling of reason concerning good and evil were considered in the actions of God and in His rulings, then creation would be evil from God. And since it is not so, we know that it is false. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage constitutes a refutation of two proposed divisions regarding the purpose of divine creation. The author first addresses the claim that God created the world for a benefit returning to the creature. He provides three arguments against this: (1) It entails circularity because the creature must exist to receive benefit, but existence itself requires the act of creation, and that act cannot be called beneficence unless the creature already exists. (2) Beneficence requires prior need or desire to be meaningful, but the creature had no such need before existence, so the supposed benefit is not true beneficence. (3) If the benefit is in this world, it is false because this world is full of suffering; if in the Hereafter, most people are disbelievers who suffer eternal punishment, so no benefit is realized. The author then rejects the second claim that God created without any purpose, calling it absurd and rationally evil. The conclusion is that if rational judgment of good and evil (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih) were valid concerning God's actions, creation itself would be deemed evil. Since it is not, this rational judgment is invalidated. The passage is a classic Ash'ari argument against the Mu'tazilite doctrine that reason can independently judge God's actions.

Key Terms
الدور
الإحسان
التحسين والتقبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God created the world primarily to benefit creatures, so His action is morally good according to human reason.
Correction: This leads to circularity and ignores that God's actions are not subject to human moral categories because creatures did not exist to need benefit.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what logical problem does claiming creation for benefit entail?
A) It implies God is needy
B) It entails circularity (al-dawr)
C) It leads to infinite regress
D) It contradicts divine omnipotence

Key Takeaway
Creation cannot be for the creature's benefit because benefit requires prior existence, leading to circularity, and the supposed benefit is not realized.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you secretly believe that God owes you something—good health, happiness, or success—because you worship Him? How does the argument that creation is not for your benefit challenge that assumption?
?
If this world is truly an 'abode of tribulation' and not a place of ultimate benefit, what are you actually living for? How does that reshape your daily priorities and attachments?
?
The passage says that beneficence requires prior need. Since you did not exist before creation, what does that imply about any claim you might make on God? Are you ready to accept that you deserve nothing from Him—and that His mercy is purely gratuitous?

Refuting the Claim of Purposeless Creation and the Conclusion
Refutation of purposeless creation and final conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming that God created without purpose is rationally absurd and why rational judgments of good and evil do not apply to God.

Core Idea
Purposeless creation would be absurd, but rational moral judgment on God is invalid.

Having refuted the idea of creation for benefit, the author now tackles the opposite claim: that God had no purpose at all. This is dismissed as absurd. The final conclusion seals the argument: rational judgments of good and evil cannot be applied to God's actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the third division and the conclusion carefully.
2
Understand 'absurdity'
The author says purposeless creation is considered 'evil according to reason'. Grasp why this is rejected.
Absurdity implies no wisdom.
3
The concluding syllogism
If rational good/evil applied, creation would be deemed evil. Since it is not, that judgment is invalid.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

The Logical Entailment of Divine Threats
Beginning of third argument: conditional and first division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first part of the third argument against rational moral judgment: why divine threats cannot be evil and the problems with attributing need to God.

Core Idea
If God's threats were rationally evil, then God would be needy—an impossibility.

This lesson introduces the third major argument in the discourse on rational moral judgment (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih). The author presents a conditional: if reason's judgment applied to God's actions, then even divine threats of punishment would be considered evil. Yet unanimously, such threats are not evil. The argument then unpacks why, starting with the first division where the servant questions the purpose of punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional premise
Notice how the argument begins with a conditional: if reason's judgment were valid for God, then threats would be evil. But they are not evil, so reason is isolated.
The consensus (bil-ittifāq) is key.
2
Understand the first division
The servant asks whether punishment is for God's benefit or his own. If for God, that implies need in God—impossible. If for himself, eternal punishment for failing to benefit himself is contradictory.
The text labels this as 'the first division' (al-qism al-awwal).
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: what does this say about the relationship between divine commands and human reason? How does this argument defend God's transcendence?
This connects to the broader theme of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : لو كان حكم العقل بالتحسين معتبراً في أفعال الله تعالى ، وفي أفعاله لقبح من الله أن يقول للعبد : إن فعلت الفحل الفلاني أو تركت الفعل الفلاني عاقبتك . وبالاتفاق هذا لا يقبح ، فوجب أن يكون العقل معزولاً في هذا الحكم . بيان الملازمة : أنه تعالي إذا قال لعبده إن فعلت الفعل الفلاني أو تركت الفعل الفلاني عاقبتك ، فللعبد أن يقول : يا إله العالمين . اتمعذبني لأجل منفعة تعود إليك ؟ أو إلى ، أو إلى ثالث . فإن عذبتني على الفعل الفلاني أو على الترك الفلاني ، لنفع يعود إليك فأنت محتاج وطالب لجر المنفعة ولدفع المضرة . وهذا محال . وأيضاً : هب أنك محتاج ولكن من البعيد أن تكون في الحاجة والفقر إلى حيث تنتفع بالأعمال الحقيرة التي أتي بها في الصلاة أو في غيرها . ( هذا هو القسم الأول ) (۱)
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أنك تعذبني على فعل كذا ، أو على ترك كذا . لأجل أن ذلك الفعل أو ذلك الترك اقتضى فوت بعض المنافع عني . فهذا متناقض . لأن حاصل الكلام يصير إلى أنه يقال للعبد : ما كان لي غرض في أمرك بالفعل الفلاني ، وفي نهيك عن الفعل الفلاني . إلا أن يحصل لك منه نفع ، أو يندفع عنك بسبه ضرر ، فلما لم تسع في تحصيل ذلك النفع لنفسك ، فأنا أعذبك أبد الآباد ، لأجل أنك قصرت في تحصيل ذلك النفع لنفسك وهذا متناقض لأن المقصود من السعي في حمله على تحصيل ذلك النفع لنفسه أن ينتفع به. وأهم المهمات له : اندفاع الضرر ، فاتصال الضرر العظيم إليه حملا له على أن يسعى في تحصيل النفع لنفسه . كلام متناقض . ومثاله : أن يقول الرجل لعبده : اجتهد في تحصيل دينار لنفسك ، لأجل أن يعود نفع ذلك الدينار إليك . فإن لم تفعل ذلك فإني أقطع أعضاءك بمقاريض من النار ، لأجل أنك قصرت في تحصيل ذلك الدينار لنفسك . ومعلوم أن هذا الكلام في غاية الفساد، وأيضاً : فلا نزاع في أنه يحسن من الله تعالى أن يتفضل بأنواع المنافع إنما الذي يتوقف حصوله على التكليف استحقاق التعظيم ، ولا شك أن حصول هذا الاستحقاق من الأمور التي ليست من المهمات الأصلية ، ولا من الحاجات الضرورية ، فإذا قصر العبد في التكاليف فحينئذ يفوته هذا الاستحقاق،
The third argument: If the ruling of reason concerning good and evil were considered in the actions of God Most High and in His actions, it would be evil for God to say to the servant: 'If you do such and such an act or leave such and such an act, I will punish you.' And by agreement this is not evil, so reason must be isolated in this judgment. Explanation of the entailment: When He Most High says to His servant: 'If you do such and such an act or leave such and such an act, I will punish you,' the servant may say: 'O God of the worlds! Do You punish me for a benefit returning to You? Or to me, or to a third? If You punish me for such an act or such an omission for a benefit returning to You, then You are needy and seeker of drawing benefit and repelling harm. And that is impossible. Also: Suppose You are needy, but it is far that You are in such need and poverty that You benefit from lowly deeds performed in prayer or otherwise.' (This is the first division.) As for the second division: That You punish me for doing such or leaving such, because that act or that omission caused the loss of some benefits for me. This is contradictory. Because the sum of the statement becomes: It is said to the servant: 'I had no purpose in commanding you to do such and such, and in forbidding you from such and such, except that you obtain benefit by it, or that harm is repelled from you by it. Since you did not strive to obtain that benefit for yourself, I will punish you forever because you fell short in obtaining that benefit for yourself.' And this is contradictory, because the purpose of striving to get him to obtain that benefit for himself is that he may benefit by it. And the most important of matters for him is the repulsion of harm. So connecting great harm to him to impel him to strive for benefit for himself is contradictory speech. Its example: A man says to his slave: 'Strive to obtain a dinar for yourself, so that the benefit of that dinar may return to you. If you do not do that, then I will cut your limbs with scissors of fire because you fell short in obtaining that dinar for yourself.' And it is known that this speech is extremely corrupt. Also: There is no dispute that it is good for God Most High to bestow various kinds of benefits. Only the attainment of merit for veneration depends on obligation. And there is no doubt that the attainment of this merit is among matters that are not of primary importance nor of necessary needs. So if the servant falls short in obligations, then he loses this merit.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument against the validity of rational moral judgment (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih) in relation to God. The argument is structured as a conditional: If reason's judgment of good and evil were valid for God, then it would be evil for God to threaten His servant with punishment for doing or not doing certain acts. But by consensus, such threats are not evil, so reason is isolated from this domain. The author then explains the entailment (bayān al-mulāzama) in two divisions. In the first division, the servant might ask whether the punishment is for God's own benefit—implying need in God, which is impossible—or for the servant's benefit, which would be contradictory because the servant's failure to benefit himself is made worse by eternal punishment. The second division is even more explicitly contradictory: the command is for the servant's own good, yet punishment is inflicted for not achieving that good, which is like a master ordering his slave to earn a dinar for himself and then cutting him with fiery scissors for failing—an obviously absurd and contradictory speech. The author concludes by noting that God may graciously bestow benefits, but the merit (istiḥqāq al-taʿẓīm) that comes through obligation is not a primary need, so its loss does not justify such punishment. This reinforces the impossibility of applying human rational ethics to divine commands.

Key Terms
التحسين والتقبيح
الحجة الثالثة
الملازمة
القسم الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that God can threaten punishment at all.
Correction: The argument only shows that if rational moral judgment applied to God, then threats would be evil. But since they are not evil, reason does not apply to God's actions. It does not deny that God threatens; it denies that human reason can judge such threats as good or evil.

Checkpoint
In the first division of the third argument, why is it impossible for God to punish for His own benefit?
A) Because God is not capable of benefiting.
B) Because that would imply need (hāja) in God, which is impossible.
C) Because the servant does not deserve punishment.
D) Because God's actions are not subject to reason.

Key Takeaway
The first division shows that divine threats cannot be rationally evil because that would require God to be needy or act contradictorily, both impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read God's warnings about punishment in the Qur'an, do you ever find yourself thinking 'that seems unfair' or 'that doesn't make sense to me'? How does this lesson's claim—that human reason cannot judge divine actions as good or evil—challenge you to submit to God's threats even when they appear harsh?
?
If God's punishment is not for His own benefit and not merely for your benefit as you perceive it, then what reason do you have to fear His threats? How does this reshape your attitude towards sin and repentance—do you obey out of fear of consequences you understand, or out of awe of a God whose ways are beyond your moral categories?
?
The argument says even entertaining the idea that God benefits from your lowly acts is absurd. Yet you often act as if your worship and obedience somehow 'do God a favor' or earn His gratitude. When in your daily life do you unconsciously assume God needs your devotion? How can you train your heart to see your acts of worship as purely dependent on His grace, not as contributions to His benefit?

The Contradiction of Self-Defeating Threats
Second division of third argument, analogy, and conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second division of the third argument, including the analogy of the master and slave, and the conclusion about the non-essential nature of merit for veneration.

Core Idea
Punishing someone for failing to benefit themselves is like cutting a slave's limbs for not earning a dinar—sheer contradiction.

The author continues the third argument by presenting the second division, which unfolds as an even more explicit contradiction. The command to the servant is for his own benefit, yet the punishment is inflicted precisely because he did not obtain that benefit. This is illustrated with a powerful analogy: a master commanding his slave to earn a dinar for himself and then cutting his limbs with fiery scissors for failing. The lesson concludes by noting that the merit for veneration that comes from obligations is not a primary need, so its loss does not justify such punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the second division
Note how the author first expresses the contradiction in abstract terms: the command is for the servant's benefit, yet punishment is for not obtaining that benefit.
The phrase 'this is contradictory' (hādhā mutanāqiḍ) appears twice.
2
Examine the analogy
The analogy of the master and slave makes the contradiction vivid: a master orders his slave to earn a dinar for himself, then punishes him for failing with fiery shears. This is 'extremely corrupt' speech.
The analogy is designed to show the absurdity of the second division.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author adds that the only thing dependent on obligation is the merit for veneration (istiḥqāq al-taʿẓīm), which is not a primary need. Losing it does not justify punishment.
This reinforces that the threat is not only contradictory but also excessive.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : لو كان حكم العقل بالتحسين معتبراً في أفعال الله تعالى ، وفي أفعاله لقبح من الله أن يقول للعبد : إن فعلت الفحل الفلاني أو تركت الفعل الفلاني عاقبتك . وبالاتفاق هذا لا يقبح ، فوجب أن يكون العقل معزولاً في هذا الحكم . بيان الملازمة : أنه تعالي إذا قال لعبده إن فعلت الفعل الفلاني أو تركت الفعل الفلاني عاقبتك ، فللعبد أن يقول : يا إله العالمين . اتمعذبني لأجل منفعة تعود إليك ؟ أو إلى ، أو إلى ثالث . فإن عذبتني على الفعل الفلاني أو على الترك الفلاني ، لنفع يعود إليك فأنت محتاج وطالب لجر المنفعة ولدفع المضرة . وهذا محال . وأيضاً : هب أنك محتاج ولكن من البعيد أن تكون في الحاجة والفقر إلى حيث تنتفع بالأعمال الحقيرة التي أتي بها في الصلاة أو في غيرها . ( هذا هو القسم الأول ) (۱)
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أنك تعذبني على فعل كذا ، أو على ترك كذا . لأجل أن ذلك الفعل أو ذلك الترك اقتضى فوت بعض المنافع عني . فهذا متناقض . لأن حاصل الكلام يصير إلى أنه يقال للعبد : ما كان لي غرض في أمرك بالفعل الفلاني ، وفي نهيك عن الفعل الفلاني . إلا أن يحصل لك منه نفع ، أو يندفع عنك بسبه ضرر ، فلما لم تسع في تحصيل ذلك النفع لنفسك ، فأنا أعذبك أبد الآباد ، لأجل أنك قصرت في تحصيل ذلك النفع لنفسك وهذا متناقض لأن المقصود من السعي في حمله على تحصيل ذلك النفع لنفسه أن ينتفع به. وأهم المهمات له : اندفاع الضرر ، فاتصال الضرر العظيم إليه حملا له على أن يسعى في تحصيل النفع لنفسه . كلام متناقض . ومثاله : أن يقول الرجل لعبده : اجتهد في تحصيل دينار لنفسك ، لأجل أن يعود نفع ذلك الدينار إليك . فإن لم تفعل ذلك فإني أقطع أعضاءك بمقاريض من النار ، لأجل أنك قصرت في تحصيل ذلك الدينار لنفسك . ومعلوم أن هذا الكلام في غاية الفساد، وأيضاً : فلا نزاع في أنه يحسن من الله تعالى أن يتفضل بأنواع المنافع إنما الذي يتوقف حصوله على التكليف استحقاق التعظيم ، ولا شك أن حصول هذا الاستحقاق من الأمور التي ليست من المهمات الأصلية ، ولا من الحاجات الضرورية ، فإذا قصر العبد في التكاليف فحينئذ يفوته هذا الاستحقاق،
The third argument: If the ruling of reason concerning good and evil were considered in the actions of God Most High and in His actions, it would be evil for God to say to the servant: 'If you do such and such an act or leave such and such an act, I will punish you.' And by agreement this is not evil, so reason must be isolated in this judgment. Explanation of the entailment: When He Most High says to His servant: 'If you do such and such an act or leave such and such an act, I will punish you,' the servant may say: 'O God of the worlds! Do You punish me for a benefit returning to You? Or to me, or to a third? If You punish me for such an act or such an omission for a benefit returning to You, then You are needy and seeker of drawing benefit and repelling harm. And that is impossible. Also: Suppose You are needy, but it is far that You are in such need and poverty that You benefit from lowly deeds performed in prayer or otherwise.' (This is the first division.) As for the second division: That You punish me for doing such or leaving such, because that act or that omission caused the loss of some benefits for me. This is contradictory. Because the sum of the statement becomes: It is said to the servant: 'I had no purpose in commanding you to do such and such, and in forbidding you from such and such, except that you obtain benefit by it, or that harm is repelled from you by it. Since you did not strive to obtain that benefit for yourself, I will punish you forever because you fell short in obtaining that benefit for yourself.' And this is contradictory, because the purpose of striving to get him to obtain that benefit for himself is that he may benefit by it. And the most important of matters for him is the repulsion of harm. So connecting great harm to him to impel him to strive for benefit for himself is contradictory speech. Its example: A man says to his slave: 'Strive to obtain a dinar for yourself, so that the benefit of that dinar may return to you. If you do not do that, then I will cut your limbs with scissors of fire because you fell short in obtaining that dinar for yourself.' And it is known that this speech is extremely corrupt. Also: There is no dispute that it is good for God Most High to bestow various kinds of benefits. Only the attainment of merit for veneration depends on obligation. And there is no doubt that the attainment of this merit is among matters that are not of primary importance nor of necessary needs. So if the servant falls short in obligations, then he loses this merit.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument against the validity of rational moral judgment (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih) in relation to God. The argument is structured as a conditional: If reason's judgment of good and evil were valid for God, then it would be evil for God to threaten His servant with punishment for doing or not doing certain acts. But by consensus, such threats are not evil, so reason is isolated from this domain. The author then explains the entailment (bayān al-mulāzama) in two divisions. In the first division, the servant might ask whether the punishment is for God's own benefit—implying need in God, which is impossible—or for the servant's benefit, which would be contradictory because the servant's failure to benefit himself is made worse by eternal punishment. The second division is even more explicitly contradictory: the command is for the servant's own good, yet punishment is inflicted for not achieving that good, which is like a master ordering his slave to earn a dinar for himself and then cutting him with fiery scissors for failing—an obviously absurd and contradictory speech. The author concludes by noting that God may graciously bestow benefits, but the merit (istiḥqāq al-taʿẓīm) that comes through obligation is not a primary need, so its loss does not justify such punishment. This reinforces the impossibility of applying human rational ethics to divine commands.

Key Terms
القسم الثاني
الاستحقاق
المهمات الأصلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that God cannot punish any disobedience.
Correction: The argument only targets punishment that is for the servant's own benefit and fails. It does not deny God's right to punish for other reasons, such as justice or vindication of His authority.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use in the second division to illustrate the contradiction?
A) A king punishing a subject for not paying taxes.
B) A father scolding his son for not studying.
C) A master telling his slave to earn a dinar and then cutting his limbs with fiery scissors for failing.
D) A teacher failing a student for not completing homework.

Key Takeaway
The second division shows that punishing a servant for failing to benefit himself is contradictory, and the loss of non-essential merit does not justify eternal punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Has there ever been a time when you expected someone to do something for their own good, but then punished or resented them when they failed? How did your reaction mirror the contradiction in the passage? This week, when you feel frustration at someone's lack of self-care, stop and ask: Am I being like the master who commands benefit but then harms for the failure?
?
The passage says that the only thing dependent on obligation is 'merit for veneration'—not essential needs. What does that say about how you view your own religious duties? Do you sometimes act as if God's commands are for God's benefit, or as if failing them brings ultimate harm beyond the loss of that merit? Pause and examine your heart: Do you truly believe that God's commands are for your good, or do you secretly think God needs your obedience?
?
Imagine you are the servant in the analogy: commanded to earn a dinar for yourself, then threatened with fiery scissors if you don't. How would you feel about the master? Now, how often do you relate to God with that same sense of threat? Are there areas of your life where you obey God out of fear of punishment rather than desire for the benefit He promises? Be honest: would you still seek God if there were no threat at all? Use this lesson to reshape your intention.

Divine Punishment and the Limits of Analogy
Refutes Mu'tazilite punishment divisions and contrasts divine vs human justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite division of punishment is flawed and how the master-servant analogy fails to apply to God.

Core Idea
God's punishment is for the servant's benefit, not His own.

The author examines possible reasons for God punishing a servant and finds each either contradictory or unjust. He then contrasts with human punishment to expose the flaw in the Mu'tazilite analogy, showing that God's actions are fundamentally different from human masters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the argument structure.
The passage begins mid-argument, so pay attention to the flow.
2
Identify the three divisions
Notice how the author presents and rejects each division: first (implied), third, and fourth.
The first division is mentioned only briefly; focus on the explicit ones.
3
Examine the master-servant analogy
Understand why human punishment is justified (master's benefit) and why that logic does not apply to God.
God cannot benefit from servants' actions.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that God's commands are for the servants' benefit, so punishment is not evil.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that rational judgments of good and evil apply to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتعذيبه عليه يقتضي إلحاق أعظم المضار به ، لأجل أنه قصر في تحصيل منفعة غير ضرورية لنفسه . ومعلوم أنه متناقض
وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يعذبه الله تعالى لأجل أن يحصل بواسطته نفع لغيره . فهذا محض الظلم ، لأن الظالم لا يفعل شيئاً إلا أن يلحق الضرر بغيره ، لأجل أن يلحق النفع بنفسه . والله متعالي عن الظلم
وأما القسم الرابع : وهو أنه تعالى يلحق العقاب بهذا العبد ، لا لشيء من هذه الأقسام الثلاثة . فهذا أيضاً محض الظلم ولا يبقى معه اعتبار البتة
بتحسين العقل وتقبيحه .
4
واعلم أن السيد إذا أمر عبده في الشاهد بفعل . فما أطاعه ، فإنه يحسن من السيد إيذاء ذلك العبد وإيلامه . وذلك لأن السيد يقول : إنما أمرتك بالفعل الفلاني لأجل أن أستفيد من ذلك الفعل ، نفعاً بنفسي فلما لم تأت بذلك الفعل ، فقد بقيت محروماً من ذلك النفع ، فأعاقبك لأجل أنك سعيت في تفويت النفع علي . وهذا في حق الله تعالى محال ، لأنه يمتنع أن ينتفع بأفعال العباد . فلم يبق إلا أن يقال : إنه تعالى إنما أمرهم ، وإنما نهاهم لمنافع عائدة إليهم. وقد بينا أنه متى كان الأمر كذلك فإنه ( لا ) (۱) يقبح من الله تعالى أن يعاقبهم على ترك الواجب ، أو على فعل الحرام ، فظهر الفرق بين البابين
His punishment of him necessitates causing the greatest harm to him, because he fell short in obtaining a non-necessary benefit for himself. And it is known that he is contradictory.
As for the third division: that God punishes him in order that through him benefit may accrue to another. This is sheer injustice, because the unjust person does not do anything except that he causes harm to another in order to bring benefit to himself. And God is exalted above injustice.
As for the fourth division: that He, exalted, inflicts punishment upon this servant, for none of these three divisions. This also is sheer injustice, and no consideration remains with it at all.
by [the principle of] rational judgment of good and evil.
4
Know that if a master commands his servant in the witnessed world to do an act, and he does not obey him, then it is good for the master to harm that servant and cause him pain. That is because the master says: 'I only commanded you to do such an act in order that I may derive benefit from that act for myself. Since you did not do that act, I have remained deprived of that benefit, so I punish you because you strove to cause me to lose the benefit.' This is impossible in the case of God, exalted, because it is impossible that He benefits from the acts of servants. So nothing remains except to say that He, exalted, only commanded them and only forbade them for benefits returning to them. And we have explained that whenever the matter is thus, then it is not evil for God, exalted, to punish them for abandoning the obligatory or for doing the forbidden. So the difference between the two cases has become clear.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position on rational judgments of good and evil (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih al-'aqli) in relation to divine punishment. The author examines the possible reasons for God's punishment of a servant and rejects all of them, then contrasts with human punishment in the empirical world (shāhid).

The passage begins by stating that if God punishes a servant for the first reason (that the servant fell short in obtaining a non-necessary benefit for himself), then it leads to a contradiction. Then it discusses the third division: that God punishes to benefit another. This is described as sheer injustice (zulm) because an unjust person harms another to benefit himself, but God is exalted above that. The fourth division is punishment for no reason at all, which is also sheer injustice.

Then the author introduces a comparison with the empirical world: a master who commands his servant and the servant disobeys. It is considered good (hasan) for the master to punish the servant because the master sought his own benefit from the servant's action. Since the servant failed to provide that benefit, the master is justified in punishing him. However, this reasoning does not apply to God because God cannot benefit from the actions of His servants. Therefore, God's commands and prohibitions are only for the benefit of the servants themselves. Since that is the case, it is not evil for God to punish them for abandoning obligations or committing prohibitions. Thus, the difference between the divine case and the human case becomes clear.

The author is arguing against the Mu'tazilite claim that actions are inherently good or evil and that God must act according to those rational judgments. Here, he shows that the analogy between human masters and God is flawed because God's motives are entirely different.

Key Terms
تعذيب (punishment)
ظلم (injustice)
نفع (benefit)
ضرر (harm)
السيد (master)
العبد (servant)
الشاهد (the witnessed world)
تحسين العقل وتقبيحه (rational judgment of good and evil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's punishment is arbitrary because it does not benefit Him or the punished.
Correction: God punishes for the servant's ultimate benefit through divine wisdom, as commands are for their own good.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the master-servant analogy in the empirical world not applicable to God?
A) Because the master can never punish the servant genuinely.
B) Because God cannot benefit from the actions of His servants.
C) Because the servant never disobeys God.
D) Because punishment is always evil.

Key Takeaway
Divine punishment differs from human punishment: human masters punish for personal benefit, while God punishes for the servant's own benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you disobey Allah, do you truly believe that His punishment is for your own benefit, or do you secretly think of Him as a harsh master? What would change in your heart if you deeply internalized that His commands are for your good? Will you start your next act of obedience with that intention?
?
This lesson shows that human masters punish for their own gain, but Allah punishes for yours. Think of a sin you struggle with. How does framing Allah's prohibition as a protection for you, not a restriction, change your desire to avoid it? What concrete step will you take today to act on that belief?
