فهذه جملة الدلائل في بيان أن حكم العقل بالتحسين والتقبيح في أحكام الله وفي أفعاله : حكم باطل ، غير ملتفت إليه. واعلم أن جماعة عظيمة من أصحاب أبي الحسن الأشعري - رحمه الله - احتجوا على إبطال القول بتحسين العقل وتقبيحه . بأن قالوا : لو قبح القتل لذاته ، لوجب أن يقبح كل قتل وكان يجب أن يقبح القتل على سبيل القصاص ، ولو حسنت المنفعة لذاتها ، لحسنت كل منفعة ولذة ، فكان يجب حسن الزنا واللواطة ، ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك، علمنا أن القول بأن الحسن حسن لذاته ، وأن القبيح قبيح لذاته قول باطل . وقالت المعتزلة : هذا الكلام غير وارد علينا ، لأنا لا نقول : إن الحسن إنما كان حسناً لذاته، وأن القبيح إنما كان قبيحاً لذاته . وإنما نقول : إن الحسن يحسن لوجوه عائلة إليه ، وإن القبيح إنما يقبح لوجوه عائدة إليه . وعند هذا قالت الأشعرية : إن التعليل بالوجوه تحليل بأمر غير مفهوم ولا معقول . ونحن نذكر تفسير هذه الوجوه بحيث يصير المراد منه مفهوماً فنقول : لا شك أن الإنسان قد يلتذ بتناول الخل ، إلا أن المقتضى لحصول تلك اللذة ليس هو تناول الخل فقط ، بل الموجب له مجموع أمور ، مثل أن يكون قد تقدم أكل الطعام الطيب الدسم ، وأن تكون المعدة قوية ، فعند تقدم ذلك الأكل يكون تناول الخل لذيذاً لمثل هذه المعدة. فعلى هذا لا نقول : إن الموجب لتلك اللذة ذات الخل ، بل الموجب مجموع هذه الأحوال والشرائط. وأيضاً : الإنسان يرغب في تناول الدواء المخصوص ، وليس المقتضى لحصول تلك الرغبة هو ذات ذلك الدواء ، وإنما المقتضى له مجموع أمور منها : حصول أخلاط مخصوصة في البدن. ومنها : أن لا توجد سائر الأدوية ومنها : ظهور آثار المرض في البدن فتصير مجموع هذه الأحوال موجباً لحصول الرغبة في شرب ذلك الدواء . وإذا عرفت هذا المعنى عرفت أنه ليس تحت فلك القمر شيء يوجب شيئاً لذاته ، ولعينه. وإنما يحصل الإيجاب والاقتضاء عند مجموع أحوال ووجوه وشرائط . إذا عرفت هذا فالمعتزلة قالوا : نحن لا نقول : إن المقتضى للقبح في القتل كونه قتلاً فقط . وإنما المقتضى مجموع أمور : وهو كونه الما واقعاً ، لا لأجل جناية سابقة ، ولا لأجل عوض لاحق. وكذا القول في سائر الأشياء الحسنة والقبيحة. فظهر بهذا أن الذين حولوا على هذه الحجة في إبطال كلام المعتزلة لم يعرفوا مذهبهم ، ولم يحيطوا بمقالتهم والله أعلم)
This is a summary of the proofs demonstrating that the ruling of the intellect by [the principle of] moral improvement and deterioration (al-taḥsīn wa al-taqbīḥ) regarding God's rulings and His actions is an invalid ruling, not to be regarded. Know that a great group of the followers of Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī - may God have mercy on him - argued for the invalidation of the statement [affirming] the intellect's [judgment of] improvement and deterioration. They said: If killing were evil in its essence (li-dhātihi), then every killing would necessarily be evil, and killing by way of retaliation (qiṣāṣ) would necessarily be evil. And if benefit were good in its essence, then every benefit and pleasure would be good, so fornication (zinā) and sodomy (liwāṭa) would necessarily be good. Since this is not the case, we know that the statement that the good is good in its essence and the evil is evil in its essence is a false statement. The Muʿtazila said: This speech does not apply to us, for we do not say that the good is good in its essence, nor that the evil is evil in its essence. Rather, we say that the good is good due to aspects (wujūh) that accrue to it, and the evil is evil due to aspects that return to it. Upon this the Ashʿariyya said: The explanation by aspects is an analysis by something not understood nor rational. And we shall mention an interpretation of these aspects such that the intended meaning becomes understandable. We say: There is no doubt that a person may enjoy consuming vinegar, but the cause (al-muqtaḍā) for the occurrence of that pleasure is not merely consuming vinegar; rather, the necessitating factor is a combination of matters (majmūʿ umūr), such as the prior consumption of rich, fatty food and a strong stomach. Upon the occurrence of that eating, consuming vinegar becomes pleasurable for such a stomach. Thus we do not say that the necessitating factor for that pleasure is the essence of vinegar; rather, the necessitating factor is the combination of these conditions and circumstances. Also, a person desires to take a particular medicine, and the cause of that desire is not the essence of that medicine; rather, the cause is a combination of matters, including the presence of particular humors (akhlāṭ) in the body, the absence of other medicines, and the appearance of symptoms of illness in the body. Then this combination of conditions becomes the necessitating factor for the desire to drink that medicine. When you understand this meaning, you understand that there is nothing under the celestial sphere (falk al-qamar) that necessitates something by its essence and its specific identity. Rather, necessitation and causation occur only upon a combination of conditions, aspects, and circumstances. Having understood this, the Muʿtazila said: We do not say that the cause of evilness in killing is simply its being killing. Rather, the cause is a combination of matters: it is an act of aggression (al-manʿ) occurring not due to a prior crime nor for a subsequent compensation. And the same applies to all other good and evil things. Thus it becomes evident that those who attacked this argument to invalidate the Muʿtazila's discourse did not know their doctrine nor did they comprehend their statement. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage presents the Ashʿari argument against the Muʿtazilite doctrine of rational good and evil (al-taḥsīn wa al-taqbīḥ), followed by the Muʿtazilite response and the author's clarification. The Ashʿaris argue that if good and evil were intrinsic to acts, then all instances of an act type would share the same moral value—e.g., all killing would be evil (including lawful retaliation) and all pleasures would be good (including illicit ones). Since this contradicts clear religious rulings, the doctrine is false. The Muʿtazila reply that they do not posit intrinsic moral values; rather, acts become good or evil due to attendant aspects (wujūh) such as context and consequences. The Ashʿaris object that such aspects are unintelligible. To refute this, the author illustrates with examples: Enjoying vinegar depends on prior conditions (rich food, strong stomach); desiring a medicine depends on bodily humors and illness. These show that nothing under the moon necessitates its effect by its essence; causation is always conditional. Applying this to ethics, the evil of killing is not due to its essense but to its being an unjust aggression (ẓulm) without prior crime or compensation. Thus the Ashʿari argument fails because it misrepresents the Muʿtazilite position. The author concludes that those who attacked the Muʿtazila did not truly understand their doctrine.

Key Terms
وجوه
مجموع أحوال وشرائط
مقتضى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes killing evil according to the Mu'tazila?
A) Its essence as killing
B) The fact that it is a prohibited act in scripture
C) A combination of aspects: being an unjust aggression without prior crime or compensation
D) The intellect's independent judgment alone

Key Takeaway
Moral judgments are contingent on a combination of aspects, not on the essence of actions; this is illustrated by analogies from everyday causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you judge an action as 'wrong', do you ever stop to consider the specific conditions that make it wrong—or do you simply label it by its name? What would it mean for your daily judgments if you consistently examined the 'combination of aspects' before condemning?
?
The author argues that even the pleasure of vinegar depends on prior conditions. Think of a recent time you felt pleasure or desire. Were you aware of the conditions that produced it? How might this awareness change how you pursue or avoid certain experiences?
?
If moral evil is not in the act itself but in the circumstances of injustice, how often do you fail to see the circumstances of others' actions—and how often do you excuse your own by 'but I had reasons'? Which of your habits needs to be re-evaluated in light of this principle?

The Mu'tazila Appeal to Necessity in Rational Good and Evil
Describes the Mu'tazila's first argument: appeal to necessity and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first type of Mu'tazila argument for rational good and evil: claiming necessity in the witnessed world and then transferring that judgment to the unseen.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila claim that good and evil are known by necessity and then analogized to the unseen.

The Mu'tazila did not rely solely on reason in the abstract; they had specific argumentative strategies. One of their key approaches was to assert that judgments of good and evil are intuitively known in this world, and then argue by analogy that the same applies to God and the unseen. This lesson explores that first type of argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first type
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the two parts: the claim of necessity and the transfer to the unseen.
2
Identify the two components
The argument has two steps: first, that good and evil are known by necessity in the witnessed world; second, that this judgment must apply to the unseen by analogy.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the Mu'tazila start with necessity. Think about potential objections to this analogical transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل العاشر في مكانة كلمات المعتزلة في القول بتحسين العقل وتقييمه. اعلم أن القوم لهم في هذا الموقف نوعان من الكلام: الأول: ادعاء الضرورة في ثبوت الحسن والقبح في الشاهد. ثم بعد تقرير هذا المقام يزعمون: أنه لما ثبت ذلك في الشاهد وجب ثبوته في الغائب. والثاني: إثبات أن الحسن يحسن لوجوه عائدة إليه، وأن القبح يقبح لوجوه عائدة إليه بالحجة والدليل.
The tenth chapter on the status of the Mu'tazila's statements regarding the assertion of intellectual good and evil. Know that these people have two types of speech in this position: The first: claiming necessity in the establishment of good and evil in the witnessed world. Then, after establishing this position, they claim that when it is established in the witnessed world, its establishment in the unseen is necessary. The second: establishing that the good is good for aspects returning to it, and that the evil is evil for aspects returning to it, by proof and evidence.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage introduces the two main argumentative strategies of the Mu'tazila concerning rational good and evil. The author states that there are two types of their discourse. The first type is an appeal to necessity (darura): they claim that judgments of good and evil are intuitively known in the witnessed world (al-shahid), and then they transfer that judgment to the unseen (al-gha'ib) by analogy. The second type is demonstrative: they argue that good and evil are inherent in actions themselves due to certain aspects, and they prove this by evidence. This sets up the subsequent detailed discussion.

Key Terms
الضرورة
الشاهد
الغائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila only use rational arguments without appealing to necessity.
Correction: In fact, their first argument is that our knowledge of good and evil is necessary and intuitive, not derived.

Checkpoint
What do the Mu'tazila claim about the knowledge of good and evil in the witnessed world?
A) It is learned through experience.
B) It is known by necessity.
C) It is only from revelation.
D) It is from consensus.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's first argument for rational good and evil relies on a claim of necessity in this world and an analogical extension to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your moral intuitions are necessary and universal, without questioning whether they apply to God? In what area of your life do you implicitly analogize from human to divine without evidence?
?
When you claim something is 'obviously good' or 'obviously evil,' are you perhaps following the same method as the Mu'tazila — asserting necessity? How might this affect your understanding of divine commands?
?
The Mu'tazila's argument depends on an unproven analogy. Where in your own belief system are you relying on analogies from your experience to claim knowledge about Allah? Are you willing to accept that such analogies might be flawed?

The Mu'tazila Proof of Inherent Good and Evil
Describes the Mu'tazila's second argument: proof of inherent good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second type of Mu'tazila argument for rational good and evil: demonstrating that good and evil are inherent in actions themselves by proof and evidence.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue that good and evil are intrinsic to actions, proven by rational evidence.

After appealing to necessity, the Mu'tazila also offered a demonstrative argument. They claimed that actions are good or evil due to aspects inherent in them, and this can be established by rational proofs. This lesson examines that second type.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the second type
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the focus on 'aspects returning to it' and 'by proof and evidence'.
2
Understand the concept of aspects
The Mu'tazila say that good is good for aspects returning to the action itself, not from an external source like divine command. This is a key difference with the Ash'ari view.
3
Compare with the first type
This second type is more rational and less reliant on analogy. Reflect on how it provides a stronger foundation.

Source Text

The tenth chapter on the status of the Mu'tazila's statements regarding the assertion of intellectual good and evil. Know that these people have two types of speech in this position: The first: claiming necessity in the establishment of good and evil in the witnessed world. Then, after establishing this position, they claim that when it is established in the witnessed world, its establishment in the unseen is necessary. The second: establishing that the good is good for aspects returning to it, and that the evil is evil for aspects returning to it, by proof and evidence.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage introduces the two main argumentative strategies of the Mu'tazila concerning rational good and evil. The author states that there are two types of their discourse. The first type is an appeal to necessity (darura): they claim that judgments of good and evil are intuitively known in the witnessed world (al-shahid), and then they transfer that judgment to the unseen (al-gha'ib) by analogy. The second type is demonstrative: they argue that good and evil are inherent in actions themselves due to certain aspects, and they prove this by evidence. This sets up the subsequent detailed discussion.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's second argument holds that good and evil are intrinsic qualities of actions, discernible by reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If good and evil are intrinsic to actions, as the Mu'tazila claim, then what does that mean for the times you have avoided doing good because you feared human judgment? Can you identify one 'aspect' of an action that you have ignored due to peer pressure? What will you do about it today?
?
Consider an action you currently consider wrong. According to this passage, evil is evil for aspects returning to it. Can you name the aspects that make it evil? If you cannot, does that mean your judgment might be based on something other than reason? What does that reveal about your moral compass?
?
The Mu'tazila claim that reason can discern good and evil. When have you trusted your reason to make a moral decision that contradicted what others said? How did that turn out? Are you willing to submit your moral intuitions to logical scrutiny like the Mu'tazila demanded?

The First Argument: Necessity of Moral Judgment
Mu'tazila present first argument from necessity with examples and defense against revelation objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's first argument from necessity for rational moral judgment and how they defend it against the objection that it comes from revelation.

Core Idea
Moral judgment is innate in the human mind, not derived from revelation.

The Mu'tazila argued that basic moral distinctions—like gratitude for kindness and blame for harm—are known by necessity to all rational beings, even those who reject religion. This passage presents their first argument, using vivid examples to show that these judgments are ingrained in the intellect itself, and then defends this claim against the objection that such judgments come from divine law.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and examples
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the two vivid examples: the rescued man and the brick thrower.
These examples illustrate the intuitive moral distinction.
2
Understand the necessity argument
The author argues that anyone with sound reason immediately knows that kindness deserves gratitude and harm deserves blame. This is presented as a necessity (ḍarūra) of the intellect.
3
Examine the defense against revelation objection
The author anticipates an objection that these judgments come from revelation. He refutes it by pointing out that even atheists and Brahmins have these intuitions, proving they are not derived from religious law.
This is a key move in the argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that these judgments are settled in the intuition of intellects. Consider how this argument establishes a foundation for rational ethics independent of revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقام الأول: وهو الذي عول عليه الأكثرون، فتقريره: أنهم قالوا: إن كل من كان سليم العقل، إذا أحسن إليه رجل حكم عقله الصريح بأنه يجب عليه شكره ومدحه، وإن من أساء إليه وضربه فإنه يحسن منه ذمه ولومه، ألا ترى أن من كان تحت يد ظالم وذلك الظالم يشمر لقتله فذهب إنسان إلى ذلك الظالم وسعى في تخليصه من يده، فإذا حصل ذلك الخلاص، فإن صريح عقل ذلك المظلوم يحكم عليه بشكر ذلك الرجل الذي سعى في تخليصه. ولو أن إنساناً رمى وجه إنسان بأجرة، فإن صريح عقله يحكم بالفرق بين ذلك الرامي وبين تلك الأجرة، فإنه يجد من عقله حسن ذم ذلك الرامي، ولا يجد من عقله البتة حسن ذم تلك الأجرة، فيثبت أن حسن المدح والذم مقرر في العقول، مركوز في القلوب. وليس لقائل أن يقول: إن هذه الأحكام تثبت بالشرع، وذلك لأن حسن هذه الأحوال وقبحها مقرر في عقل من لم يؤمن بالله ولا باليوم الآخر، ولو كان هذا الحكم مستفاداً من الشرائع، لكان الدهري والبرهمي وجب أن لا يحصل في عقولهما هذه الأحكام، وحيث علمنا بالضرورة حصول هذه الأحكام في عقول جميع الخلق، علمنا أن هذا الحكم غير مأخوذ من الشريعة. وأيضاً: إذا رأينا العقلاء يذمون على بعض الأحوال ويمدحون على بعضها. فإذا سألناهم وقلنا لهم: لم مدحتم هذا وذممتم ذاك؟ قالوا: إن هذا أحسن إلينا، وذاك أساء إلينا. وهذا يدل على أنهم كما علموا ببديهة العقل حسن المدح والذم، فكذلك علموا ببديهة العقل أن المقتضى لحسن ذلك المدح هو الإحسان، وإن المقتضى لحسن ذلك الذم هو الإساءة، فيثبت بهذه التنبيهات: أن هذه الأحكام مقررة في بداهة العقول. وهذا هو الوجه الأول.
As for the first position, which the majority relied upon, its exposition is that they said: Whoever is of sound intellect, if a man does good to him, his clear intellect judges that he must thank and praise him; and whoever does evil to him and strikes him, then it is good from him to blame and reproach him. Do you not see that if someone is under the power of an oppressor and that oppressor prepares to kill him, and a person goes to that oppressor and strives to rescue him from his hand, and when that rescue is accomplished, then the clear intellect of that oppressed person judges that he must thank that man who strove to rescue him? And if a person throws a brick at the face of a person, his clear intellect judges the difference between that thrower and that brick, for he finds from his intellect the goodness of blaming that thrower, and he does not find from his intellect at all the goodness of blaming that brick. Thus it is established that the goodness of praise and blame is settled in minds, ingrained in hearts. And no one can say: These judgments are established by revelation, because the goodness and evil of these situations is settled in the intellect of one who does not believe in God or the Last Day. And if this judgment were derived from religious laws, then the atheist and the Brahmin would necessarily not have these judgments in their intellects. But since we know by necessity that these judgments occur in the intellects of all creatures, we know that this judgment is not taken from revelation. Also: If we see rational people blaming some situations and praising others, and we ask them: Why did you praise this and blame that? They say: This one did good to us, and that one did evil to us. This indicates that just as they know by intellectual intuition the goodness of praise and blame, so too they know by intellectual intuition that the cause of that praise is the goodness, and that the cause of that blame is the evil. Thus, by these indications, it is established that these judgments are settled in the intuition of intellects. And this is the first argument.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage presents the first type of Mu'tazila argument in detail. It is a claim of necessity (darura) based on common human experience. The author explains that anyone with sound reason instinctively knows that kindness deserves gratitude and harm deserves blame. He gives two examples: a rescued person feels obliged to thank his rescuer, and a victim of aggression naturally blames the aggressor, not the object used. This shows that moral judgments are innate. Then he anticipates an objection: perhaps these judgments come from revelation. He refutes it by pointing out that even atheists and those who reject religion have these intuitions, so they cannot be derived from religious law. Additionally, when we ask people why they praise or blame, they cite the action itself (good or harm), proving the judgment is intuitive. The author concludes that these judgments are firmly rooted in the innate intellect. This is the first of their two methods.

Key Terms
حسن المدح والذم
الضرورة
الدهري
البرهمي
بديهة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila deny any role for revelation in ethics.
Correction: The Mu'tazila only argue that basic moral judgments are known rationally before revelation; revelation can confirm or add detail but does not originate these primary intuitions.

Checkpoint
What example does the author give to show that moral judgment is not derived from revelation?
A) A person who is rescued thanks his rescuer
B) An atheist and a Brahmin have moral intuitions
C) People blame those who harm them
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's first argument establishes that moral judgments of good and evil are known by intellectual necessity, independent of revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own intellect immediately tells you that gratitude is due to a benefactor, why do you often fail to thank Allah for His countless blessings? What specific act of gratitude will you commit today to change that?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that even an atheist knows that blaming a wrongdoer is good. When someone harms you, do you seek justice out of a sense of moral duty or out of personal vengeance? How can you align your response with your innate moral sense?
?
The passage says people praise because of the goodness of the action itself, not because of any external command. When you perform good deeds, do you do them for their inherent goodness or for the sake of appearing righteous? What would it take for you to act solely from the pure recognition of good?

Innate Moral Knowledge: The Atheist and the Blind Man
Second Mu'tazila argument for intuitive moral knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila use a controlled scenario to argue that moral knowledge is intuitive and independent of external motives.

Core Idea
Moral intuition is proven by stripping away all external motives.

The Mu'tazila aim to show that our knowledge of good and evil is not learned from revelation or society, but is built into our intellect. To demonstrate this, they construct a thought experiment involving an atheist helper — eliminating all possible ulterior motives — and ask: why does he still feel compelled to help? This passage presents that argument, which is foundational to the Mu'tazila ethical theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the detailed scenario and the list of motives eliminated.
The passage is cut off, but the argument structure is complete.
2
Identify the three eliminated motives
The author explicitly rules out material reward, praise, and religious reward. Notice how each is eliminated by the details of the scenario.
3
Reflect on the choice of an atheist
Why use a 'dahri' (materialist) who denies God and the afterlife? Think about what this choice accomplishes in the argument.
It ensures that religious reward cannot be a motive.
4
Consider the implication for moral knowledge
With all external motives removed, the only remaining explanation is an innate, intuitive sense that helping is good. How does this support the claim that moral knowledge is 'badihi' (intuitive)?
5
Connect to the broader debate
This argument is part of the Mu'tazila case for rational ethics. Think about how it contrasts with views that ground morality in revelation or social convention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: في بيان أن العلم بثبوت الحسن والقبح علم بديهي: أنا إذا فرضنا إنساناً دهرياً ينكر الإله والنبوة، وفرضنا أنه كان ماراً في صحراء خالية عن الناس، فاتفق أنه عبر على إنسان أعمى، ورآه في نوع من أنواع البلاء، مشرفاً على الهلاك، فإنه قد يرق قلبه عليه، ويحمله عقله على الإحسان إليه. فههنا رغبته في الإحسان إليه ليست لطلب الجزاء، فإن ذلك الرجل فقير، وليس لأجل الطمع في الثناء، لأن ذلك الرجل أعمى لا يعرفه، ولم يحضر هناك إنسان آخر، حتى يقال: إنه يروي تلك الواقعة ليمدحه سائر الناس، وليس أيضاً لأجل الطمع في تحصيل الثواب، لأن ذلك الإنسان دهري، منكر للإله والنبوة والمعاد. فلما حصلت الرغبة العظيمة في الإحسان
The second argument: In explaining that the knowledge of the establishment of good and evil is intuitive knowledge: If we suppose a materialist (dahri) person who denies God and prophethood, and suppose he passes through a desert empty of people, and he comes across a blind person in some type of affliction, on the verge of destruction, then his heart may soften towards him, and his intellect drives him to do good to him. So here his desire to do good to him is not for the sake of seeking recompense, for that man is poor, nor for the sake of coveting praise, because that blind man does not know him, and there is no other person present such that it could be said that he will narrate the incident for other people to praise him, nor is it for the sake of coveting the attainment of reward, because that person is a materialist, denier of God, prophethood, and the afterlife. When the great desire for doing good occurs...
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument of the Mu'tazila. It aims to demonstrate that knowledge of good and evil is intuitive (badihi). The author constructs a scenario: an atheist (dahri) who denies God, prophethood, and the afterlife, walking alone in a desert, encounters a blind person in severe distress. The atheist's heart softens and his intellect compels him to help. The author then analyzes the motivation: it cannot be for material reward because the blind man is poor; it cannot be for praise because the blind man does not know him and there are no witnesses; it cannot be for religious reward because the atheist does not believe in the afterlife. Therefore, the only remaining motive is an innate, intuitive sense that helping is good. This proves that moral judgments are inherent in the intellect, not derived from external factors. The argument is cut off here, but the conclusion is implied: this demonstrates the intuitive nature of moral knowledge.

Key Terms
الدهري
بديهي
الإحسان
الثواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the second argument is a separate proof from the first; in fact, both aim to show innateness, but the second uses a more controlled scenario eliminating external motives.
Correction: The second argument complements the first by providing a scenario that isolates innate moral motivation from all possible external rewards.

Checkpoint
Why does the author emphasize that the blind man does not know the helper and there are no witnesses?
A) To show that the helper cannot be motivated by praise
B) To prove the helper is a liar
C) To argue that the helper is insane
D) To suggest that the blind man is ungrateful

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use the example of an atheist helping a blind man to argue that moral goodness is known intuitively, not derived from external rewards or praise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you did a good deed expecting something in return—praise, reward, or even just a thanks? What would it take for you to help someone purely because it is good, with no one watching and no possibility of gain?
?
The atheist in the argument still felt compelled to help. If even a denier of God cannot escape the call of goodness, how can you, who believes in the All-Seeing, ever ignore that call? Will you commit to acting on that inner moral sense this week, even if it costs you?
?
You have been shown that moral knowledge is innate. What excuse do you have for the times you have deliberately suppressed that knowledge for convenience? Are you ready to repent and realign your actions with the truth inscribed on your heart?

Refutation of Revelation-Only View and First Proof
First proof and refutation of revelation-only view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that knowledge of good and evil is not from revelation, and the first proof based on universal praise and blame.

Core Idea
Moral judgments are rooted in reason, not revelation.

In this lesson, we encounter the beginning of the author's argument against the view that moral knowledge comes only from revelation. He presents a powerful observation: even atheists possess judgments of good and evil. Then he offers the first of three proofs, drawing on the universal practice of praising beneficence and blaming harm.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
The author states that praise and blame are ingrained in minds. He refutes the claim that these come from revelation by noting that even non-believers have them.
2
Study the first proof
Ask rational people why they praise or blame; they refer to the act itself (good or harm). This shows the ground is intrinsic.
3
Connect to the conclusion
This establishes that these judgments are ingrained in the start of minds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يجد من عقله البتة حسن ذم تلك الأجرة ، فيثبت أن حسن المدح والذم مقرر في العقول ، مركوز في القلوب وليس لقائل أن يقول : إن هذه الأحكام تثبت بالشرع ، وذلك لأن حسن هذه الأحوال وقبحها مقرر في عقل من لم يؤمن بالله ولا باليوم الآخر ولو كان هذا الحكم مستفاداً من الشرائع، لكان الدهري والبرهمي ، وجب أن لا يحصل في عقولهما هذه الأحكام وحيث علمنا بالضرورة حصول هذه الأحكام في عقول جميع الخلق ، علمنا أن هذا الحكم غير مأخوذ من الشريعة . وأيضاً : إذا رأينا العقلاء يذمون على بعض الأحوال ويمدحون على بعضها . فإذا سألناهم وقلنا لهم : لم مدحتم هذا ودمتم ذاك ؟ قالوا : إن هذا احسن إلينا ، وذاك أساء إلينا. وهذا يدل على أنهم كما علموا ببدية العقل حسن المدح والذم فكذلك علموا ببديهة العقل، أن المقتضى لحسن ذلك المدح هو الإحسان ، وإن المقتضى لحسن ذلك اللم هو الإساءة ، فيثبت بهذه التنبيهات : أن هذه الأحكام مقررة في بداءه العقول . ( وهذا هو الوجه الأول ) (1) . الوجه الثاني : في بيان أن العلم بثبوت الحسن والقبح علم بديي : أنا إذا فرضنا إنساناً دهرياً ينكر الإله والنبوة ، وفرضنا أنه كان ماراً في صحراء خالية عن الناس ، فاتفق أنه عبر على إنسان أعمي ، ورآه في نوع من أنواع البلاء ، مشرفاً على الهلاك ، فإنه قد يرق قلبه عليه ، ويحمله عقله على الإحسان إليه . فههنا رغبته في الإحسان إليه ليست لطلب الجزاء ، فإن ذلك الرجل فقير ، وليس لأجل الطمع في الثناء . لأن ذلك الرجل أعمى لا يعرفه ، ولم يحضر هناك إنسان آخر ، حتى يقال : إنه يروي تلك الواقعة ليمدحه سائر الناس ، وليس أيضاً لأجل الطمع في تحصيل الثواب لأن ذلك الإنسان دهري ، منكر للإله والنبوة والمعاد. فلما حصلت الرغبة العظيمة في الإحسان إلى ذلك الضعيف . وانتفت سائر الوجوه ، علمنا أن الداعي له إلى ذلك الإحسان مجرد ما تقرر في عقله من أن الإحسان حسن ، وذلك يدل على أن شهادات العقول متطابقة على حسن الإحسان ، وقبح الإساءة . الوجه الثالث : في تقرير هذا المعنى: أنه إذا قيل لإنسان : إن صدقت أعطيناك ديناراً ، وإن كذبت أعطيناك ديناراً ، وعلم بالضرورة أن المنافع الحاصلة من هذا الصدق ومن هذا الكذب متساوية في الدنيا والآخرة ، وأنه ليس لأحدهما زيادة أثر في المطالب والمنافع ، فإن الإنسان في هذه الحالة لا بد وأن يختار الصدق ، وأن يحترز عن الكذب ، ولولا أن العلم يكون الصدق حسناً من حيث أنه صدق ، وأن العلم يكون الكذب قبيحاً من حيث إنه كذب علم ضروري لازم للعقول والأفهام، لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، فيثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة : أن العلم بحسن هذه الأشياء ، ويقبحها : علم ضروري
He does not find in his intellect at all the goodness of blaming that wage, so it is established that the goodness of praise and blame is settled in minds, ingrained in hearts. And no one can say: these judgments are established by revelation; because the goodness and badness of these states is settled in the mind of one who does not believe in God nor the Last Day. And if this judgment were derived from religions, then the atheist and the Brahmin would necessarily not have these judgments in their minds. And since we know by necessity the occurrence of these judgments in the minds of all creation, we know that this judgment is not taken from revelation. Also: when we see rational people blame some states and praise others, and we ask them: 'Why did you praise this and blame that?' They say: 'This one did good to us, and that one did evil to us.' This indicates that just as they know by the start of reason the goodness of praise and blame, likewise they know by the start of reason that the ground for the goodness of that praise is beneficence, and the ground for the goodness of that blame is harm. So by these indications it is established that these judgments are settled in the start of minds. (This is the first proof.) The second proof: in clarifying that knowledge of the existence of good and evil is self-evident knowledge: we suppose an atheist who denies God and prophethood, and we suppose he was passing through a desert devoid of people, and it happened that he came upon a blind person in some kind of affliction, on the verge of destruction; then his heart may feel compassion for him, and his reason impels him to do good to him. Here his desire to do good to him is not for seeking reward, for that person is poor; nor for greed of praise, because that person is blind and does not know him, and there is no other person present such that it could be said that he will narrate the incident so that other people praise him; nor also for greed of gaining recompense, because that person is an atheist, denying God, prophethood, and the afterlife. So when the great desire to do good to that weak person occurred, and all other reasons were negated, we know that the motivator for that beneficence is merely what is established in his mind that beneficence is good. That indicates that the testimonies of minds are in agreement on the goodness of beneficence and the badness of harm. The third proof: in establishing this meaning: if one is told: 'If you are truthful I will give you a dinar, and if you lie I will give you a dinar,' and it is known by necessity that the benefits resulting from this truthfulness and this lie are equal in this world and the Hereafter, and that neither has any extra effect in goals and benefits; then the person in this situation necessarily chooses truthfulness and avoids lying. And were it not that the knowledge that truthfulness is good insofar as it is truthfulness, and that knowledge that lying is bad insofar as it is lying, is necessary knowledge inherent in minds and understandings, it would not be so. By these three proofs it is established that the knowledge of the goodness of these things and their badness is necessary knowledge.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs that knowledge of good and evil (al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ) is innate and necessary, not derived from revelation. The author begins by responding to the objection that these judgments come from revelation, noting that even atheists and polytheists have them, so the judgments must be pre-rational. Then he enumerates three proofs. The first proof (al-wajh al-awwal) is that rational people universally praise beneficence and blame harm, and when asked why, they cite the act itself—showing the ground for judgment is intrinsic. The second proof illustrates with a hypothetical atheist who spontaneously helps a blind stranger with no worldly or otherworldly motive, proving the impulse to see beneficence as good is innate. The third proof shows that when reward is equal for truth and falsehood, one still chooses truth, indicating that truthfulness is perceived as good in itself. The conclusion is that the knowledge of good and evil is necessary (ḍarūrī), not acquired from revelation.

حسن
قبح
بديهة العقل
شرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moral knowledge comes only from revelation.
Correction: The author shows that even atheists have moral judgments, so they must be innate, not derived from revelation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why can't moral judgments be solely from revelation?
A) Because even atheists have them.
B) Because they are taught in schools.
C) Because they vary by culture.
D) Because they are in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that the basis for praise and blame is found in reason itself, not in religious texts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone being treated unjustly, do you need to consult a religious text to know it's wrong, or does something inside you immediately rebel? What does that inner rebellion reveal about the source of your moral knowledge? Will you trust that innate sense more today?
?
Think of a time you praised someone for doing you good. Did you first check a scripture to decide whether to praise them? Why or why not? If you praised them spontaneously, what does that tell you about the naturalness of moral judgment? Will you acknowledge that your heart already knows good and evil?
?
The passage argues that even atheists have moral judgments. If you meet someone who denies God yet insists on justice, what does that say about the reach of moral knowledge? Does it challenge your assumption that morality comes only from faith? How will you respond to such a person, knowing that their moral sense is a sign of the same reason God placed in all humans?

Second Proof: The Atheist's Compassion
Second proof using the example of an atheist helping a stranger

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that knowledge of good and evil is innate, illustrated by the example of an atheist helping a blind stranger.

Core Idea
Even an atheist's spontaneous compassion shows that goodness is known by reason.

The author presents a vivid scenario: an atheist in a desert, seeing a blind man about to die, feels compelled to help—with no expectation of reward or praise. This, he argues, proves that the judgment that beneficence is good is innate.

Guided Path
1
Read the scenario
Read the hypothetical situation of an atheist encountering a blind man in distress.
2
Note the elimination of other motives
The author eliminates any possible worldly or otherworldly motive for helping.
3
Conclude the proof
The only remaining explanation is that the atheist's reason recognizes beneficence as good inherently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

D) Out of fear

Key Takeaway
The atheist's compassion demonstrates that the goodness of beneficence is known by reason alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you did a good deed when no one was watching and no reward was possible. What truly moved you? If you were an atheist, would that same impulse still be there? This lesson forces you to examine whether your morality depends on belief or is anchored in reason itself. What do you find?
?
The atheist in the proof acts on reason alone. But you, as a believer, have additional motives. Could your reliance on divine reward or fear of punishment be masking a deeper truth—that you already know good by reason? Are you doing good because it is inherently good, or only because Allah commanded? Be honest: which motive is stronger in your daily choices?
?
If the knowledge of good and evil is self-evident, why do you sometimes struggle to do what you know is good? Is your reason clouded by desires? This lesson challenges you to align your actions with your innate moral awareness. What is the one area today where you can act purely because it is good, without expecting anything in return?

Third Proof: Choosing Truth Over Falsehood
Third proof using equal reward for truth and falsehood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that knowledge of good and evil is innate, using the example of choosing truth when reward is equal.

Core Idea
Even when rewards are equal, we choose truth because it is inherently good.

The author's third proof is a clever thought experiment: if you are promised the same dinar for truth or lie, you will still choose truth. This shows that truthfulness is perceived as good in itself, and lying as bad, apart from consequences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Read the scenario where a person is offered the same reward for truth or lie.
2
Note that rewards are equal
The benefits in this world and the next are identical, so no external incentive favors one over the other.
3
Conclude the proof
The inevitable choice of truth over falsehood shows that the knowledge of their moral value is innate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يجد من عقله البتة حسن ذم تلك الأجرة ، فيثبت أن حسن المدح والذم مقرر في العقول ، مركوز في القلوب وليس لقائل أن يقول : إن هذه الأحكام تثبت بالشرع ، وذلك لأن حسن هذه الأحوال وقبحها مقرر في عقل من لم يؤمن بالله ولا باليوم الآخر ولو كان هذا الحكم مستفاداً من الشرائع، لكان الدهري والبرهمي ، وجب أن لا يحصل في عقولهما هذه الأحكام وحيث علمنا بالضرورة حصول هذه الأحكام في عقول جميع الخلق ، علمنا أن هذا الحكم غير مأخوذ من الشريعة . وأيضاً : إذا رأينا العقلاء يذمون على بعض الأحوال ويمدحون على بعضها . فإذا سألناهم وقلنا لهم : لم مدحتم هذا ودمتم ذاك ؟ قالوا : إن هذا احسن إلينا ، وذاك أساء إلينا. وهذا يدل على أنهم كما علموا ببدية العقل حسن المدح والذم فكذلك علموا ببديهة العقل، أن المقتضى لحسن ذلك المدح هو الإحسان ، وإن المقتضى لحسن ذلك اللم هو الإساءة ، فيثبت بهذه التنبيهات : أن هذه الأحكام مقررة في بداءه العقول . ( وهذا هو الوجه الأول ) (1) . الوجه الثاني : في بيان أن العلم بثبوت الحسن والقبح علم بديي : أنا إذا فرضنا إنساناً دهرياً ينكر الإله والنبوة ، وفرضنا أنه كان ماراً في صحراء خالية عن الناس ، فاتفق أنه عبر على إنسان أعمي ، ورآه في نوع من أنواع البلاء ، مشرفاً على الهلاك ، فإنه قد يرق قلبه عليه ، ويحمله عقله على الإحسان إليه . فههنا رغبته في الإحسان إليه ليست لطلب الجزاء ، فإن ذلك الرجل فقير ، وليس لأجل الطمع في الثناء . لأن ذلك الرجل أعمى لا يعرفه ، ولم يحضر هناك إنسان آخر ، حتى يقال : إنه يروي تلك الواقعة ليمدحه سائر الناس ، وليس أيضاً لأجل الطمع في تحصيل الثواب لأن ذلك الإنسان دهري ، منكر للإله والنبوة والمعاد. فلما حصلت الرغبة العظيمة في الإحسان إلى ذلك الضعيف . وانتفت سائر الوجوه ، علمنا أن الداعي له إلى ذلك الإحسان مجرد ما تقرر في عقله من أن الإحسان حسن ، وذلك يدل على أن شهادات العقول متطابقة على حسن الإحسان ، وقبح الإساءة . الوجه الثالث : في تقرير هذا المعنى: أنه إذا قيل لإنسان : إن صدقت أعطيناك ديناراً ، وإن كذبت أعطيناك ديناراً ، وعلم بالضرورة أن المنافع الحاصلة من هذا الصدق ومن هذا الكذب متساوية في الدنيا والآخرة ، وأنه ليس لأحدهما زيادة أثر في المطالب والمنافع ، فإن الإنسان في هذه الحالة لا بد وأن يختار الصدق ، وأن يحترز عن الكذب ، ولولا أن العلم يكون الصدق حسناً من حيث أنه صدق ، وأن العلم يكون الكذب قبيحاً من حيث إنه كذب علم ضروري لازم للعقول والأفهام، لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، فيثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة : أن العلم بحسن هذه الأشياء ، ويقبحها : علم ضروري
He does not find in his intellect at all the goodness of blaming that wage, so it is established that the goodness of praise and blame is settled in minds, ingrained in hearts. And no one can say: these judgments are established by revelation; because the goodness and badness of these states is settled in the mind of one who does not believe in God nor the Last Day. And if this judgment were derived from religions, then the atheist and the Brahmin would necessarily not have these judgments in their minds. And since we know by necessity the occurrence of these judgments in the minds of all creation, we know that this judgment is not taken from revelation. Also: when we see rational people blame some states and praise others, and we ask them: 'Why did you praise this and blame that?' They say: 'This one did good to us, and that one did evil to us.' This indicates that just as they know by the start of reason the goodness of praise and blame, likewise they know by the start of reason that the ground for the goodness of that praise is beneficence, and the ground for the goodness of that blame is harm. So by these indications it is established that these judgments are settled in the start of minds. (This is the first proof.) The second proof: in clarifying that knowledge of the existence of good and evil is self-evident knowledge: we suppose an atheist who denies God and prophethood, and we suppose he was passing through a desert devoid of people, and it happened that he came upon a blind person in some kind of affliction, on the verge of destruction; then his heart may feel compassion for him, and his reason impels him to do good to him. Here his desire to do good to him is not for seeking reward, for that person is poor; nor for greed of praise, because that person is blind and does not know him, and there is no other person present such that it could be said that he will narrate the incident so that other people praise him; nor also for greed of gaining recompense, because that person is an atheist, denying God, prophethood, and the afterlife. So when the great desire to do good to that weak person occurred, and all other reasons were negated, we know that the motivator for that beneficence is merely what is established in his mind that beneficence is good. That indicates that the testimonies of minds are in agreement on the goodness of beneficence and the badness of harm. The third proof: in establishing this meaning: if one is told: 'If you are truthful I will give you a dinar, and if you lie I will give you a dinar,' and it is known by necessity that the benefits resulting from this truthfulness and this lie are equal in this world and the Hereafter, and that neither has any extra effect in goals and benefits; then the person in this situation necessarily chooses truthfulness and avoids lying. And were it not that the knowledge that truthfulness is good insofar as it is truthfulness, and that knowledge that lying is bad insofar as it is lying, is necessary knowledge inherent in minds and understandings, it would not be so. By these three proofs it is established that the knowledge of the goodness of these things and their badness is necessary knowledge.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs that knowledge of good and evil (al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ) is innate and necessary, not derived from revelation. The author begins by responding to the objection that these judgments come from revelation, noting that even atheists and polytheists have them, so the judgments must be pre-rational. Then he enumerates three proofs. The first proof (al-wajh al-awwal) is that rational people universally praise beneficence and blame harm, and when asked why, they cite the act itself—showing the ground for judgment is intrinsic. The second proof illustrates with a hypothetical atheist who spontaneously helps a blind stranger with no worldly or otherworldly motive, proving the impulse to see beneficence as good is innate. The third proof shows that when reward is equal for truth and falsehood, one still chooses truth, indicating that truthfulness is perceived as good in itself. The conclusion is that the knowledge of good and evil is necessary (ḍarūrī), not acquired from revelation.

كذب
علم ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moral choices are always based on consequences or rewards.
Correction: The third proof shows that even when consequences are equal, the intrinsic moral quality of truthfulness guides choice, indicating innate knowledge.

Checkpoint

A) Because truth brings more reward
B) Because lying is forbidden by religion
C) Because truth is perceived as good in itself
D) Because of social pressure

The Third Proof: Good and Evil Are Known by Necessity
Sanusi presents a thought experiment proving moral judgments are necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the thought experiment that demonstrates the rational necessity of moral judgments, independent of outcomes.

Core Idea
Moral judgments are rationally necessary, not based on consequences.

In this lesson, we examine the third proof that good and evil are known by necessity. The author presents a clever thought experiment: even when truth and falsehood yield identical rewards, reason still compels us to choose truth. This shows that moral cognition is inherent in the intellect, not derived from external benefits.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: a conditional scenario followed by a conclusion.
The argument is concise; focus on the logical flow.
2
Understand the thought experiment
Imagine being told you'll receive a dinar whether you tell the truth or lie, with equal worldly and otherworldly benefits. Why would you still choose truth?
The experiment isolates the inherent moral judgment from any external incentive.
3
Reflect on the inference
The fact that you must choose truth implies that your intellect recognizes truth as good and falsehood as evil, independent of consequences. This is a necessary judgment.
This inference is the core of the proof.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The author concludes that this third wajh, along with the previous two, establishes that knowledge of good and evil is necessary (daruri) for all rational beings.
The proof is cumulative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : في تقرير هذا المعنى: أنه إذا قيل لإنسان : إن صدقت أعطيناك ديناراً ، وإن كذبت أعطيناك ديناراً ، وعلم بالضرورة أن المنافع الحاصلة من هذا الصدق ومن هذا الكذب متساوية في الدنيا والآخرة ، وأنه ليس لأحدهما زيادة أثر في المطالب والمنافع ، فإن الإنسان في هذه الحالة لا بد وأن يختار الصدق ، وأن يحترز عن الكذب ، ولولا أن العلم يكون الصدق حسناً من حيث أنه صدق ، وأن العلم يكون الكذب قبيحاً من حيث إنه كذب علم ضروري لازم للعقول والأفهام، لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، فيثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة : أن العلم بحسن هذه الأشياء ، ويقبحها : علم ضروري
The third aspect: in establishing this meaning: that if it is said to a person: 'If you tell the truth, we will give you a dinar; and if you lie, we will give you a dinar', and it is known by necessity that the benefits resulting from this truth and from this lie are equal in this world and the Hereafter, and that neither one has an additional effect in objectives and benefits, then the person in this situation must choose truth and avoid falsehood; and were it not for the fact that knowledge that truth is good insofar as it is truth, and knowledge that falsehood is evil insofar as it is falsehood, is necessary knowledge inherent in intellects and understandings, it would not be so. Thus it is established by these three aspects that knowledge of the goodness of these things and their evil is necessary knowledge.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument (wajh) for the rational necessity of knowing good and evil. The argument begins with a thought experiment: imagine a person is told that whether he speaks truth or falsehood, he will receive the same reward (a dinar) in this world and the Hereafter. The benefits are identical, so there is no external incentive. Despite this, the person will necessarily choose truth and avoid falsehood. This shows that the judgment that truth is good and falsehood is evil is not based on consequences but on an inherent rational cognition. The author concludes that this cognition is necessary for all rational beings, and thus knowledge of good and evil is necessary (daruri) according to intellect.

Misconception: Moral judgments are purely conventional or based on social custom or revealed law.
Correction: This thought experiment shows that even without any external benefit or revealed text, reason necessarily judges truth as good and falsehood as evil. Thus moral judgments are rationally necessary, not merely conventional.

The First Proof: Goodness and Evil Are Intrinsic to Actions
First proof: intrinsic moral values rest on the nature of acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational proof that good and evil are intrinsic to actions themselves, and therefore apply to God.

Core Idea
Goodness is known by recognizing the nature of the act itself.

In this passage, the author presents the first of two rational proofs that moral categories apply to God. The proof argues that truth is good because it is truth, and falsehood is evil because it is falsehood — not because of any external factor. This intrinsic connection means that wherever truth exists, it is good, including when it comes from God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall argument. Then refer to the translation to ensure understanding of the claim and proof.
Notice the repeated structure: 'truth is good because it is truth'.
2
Understand the proof of intrinsic value
The proof shows that knowledge of goodness depends solely on knowing the act as truth or falsehood, not on any other factor. This shows the cause of goodness is the act's nature.
The proof uses the example of truth and falsehood, but also mentions injustice.
3
Apply the principle to God
Since the cause (the act's nature) is universal, wherever the act exists, its moral value follows. Therefore, truth from God is good, falsehood from God is evil.
This step uses the principle: 'wherever the effective cause exists, the effect must exist'.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument is important for Islamic theology: it affirms that moral categories are objective and not subject to divine whim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني : أن نقول : لما ثبت هذا المعنى ، وجب أن يبقى هذا الحسن والقبح في حق الله تعالى ( والذي يدل عليه ) وجهان : الأول : إن الصدق إنما حسن لكونه صدقاً ، وأن الكذب إنما قبح لكونه كذباً ، وأن الظلم إنما قبح لكونه ظلماً . والدليل عليه : أن من عرف كون الصدق صدقاً عرف كونه حسناً ، وإن جهل سائر الاعتبارات ، وإن لم يعرف كونه صدقاً لم يعرف كونه حسناً ، وإن عرف سائر الاعتبارات . وكذا القول في قبح الكذب ، وقبح الظلم . ولما كان العلم بالحسن والقبح دائراً مع العلم بهذه الوجوه والاعتبارات ، وجب أن يكون المقتضى للحسن والقبح هو هذه الوجوه والاعتبارات . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : وجب أن يكون الصدق حسناً من الله ، وأن يكون قبيحاً منه لما ثبت أنه أينما حصل المؤثر والموجب . وجب أن يحصل الأثر . الثاني : إن صريح العقل كما يحكم بقبح الظلم والكذب من الواحد منا ، فكذلك يحكم بقبحهما من الله تعالى ، بل نقول : إن قبحهما من الله تعالى أكـد في العقول لأن الواحد منا قد يظلم ويكذب، إما لجهله أو لحاجته . والجهل والحاجة عذر في ذلك الفعل من بعض الوجوه . أما الله تعالى فإنه منزه عن الجهل والعجز والحاجة ، فكان حكم العقل بقبح الكذب والظلم منه أكد وأقوى من حكمه بقبحه لهما من العبد . فيثبت أن هذا الحكم كما هو ثابت في الشاهد ثابت في الغائب . فهذا جملة الكلام في تقرير كلام القائلين بأن هذه الأحكام معلومة بالضرورة
The second station: we say: Since this meaning is established, it is necessary that this goodness and evil apply to God Almighty. That which indicates it is two aspects: The first: Verily, truth is good only because it is truth, and falsehood is evil only because it is falsehood, and injustice is evil only because it is injustice. The proof of it: that whoever knows that truth is truth knows it is good, even if he is ignorant of all other considerations; and if he does not know it is truth, he does not know it is good, even if he knows all other considerations. And likewise the statement concerning the evil of falsehood and the evil of injustice. And since knowledge of good and evil revolves with knowledge of these aspects and considerations, it must be that the cause of goodness and evil is these aspects and considerations. And if this is established, we say: It must be that truth is good from God, and that falsehood is evil from Him, because since it is established that wherever the effective cause exists, the effect must exist. The second: Verily, clear reason just as it judges the evil of injustice and falsehood from one of us, so likewise it judges their evil from God Almighty. Rather, we say: Their evil from God Almighty is more confirmed in intellects because one of us may commit injustice and falsehood either due to ignorance or need. Ignorance and need are an excuse for that action in some respects. As for God Almighty, He is transcendent above ignorance, incapacity, and need. Thus reason's judgment of the evil of falsehood and injustice from Him is more confirmed and stronger than its judgment of their evil from the servant. So it is established that this judgment, just as it is established in the witnessed (al-shahid), is established in the absent (al-gha'ib). This is the sum of speech in presenting the words of those who say that these judgments are known by necessity.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a second station (maqam) argument that the moral categories of good and evil apply to God. The author gives two proofs. The first proof: The goodness of truth and evil of falsehood and injustice are intrinsic to the actions themselves, not dependent on external factors. The proof is that knowledge of goodness follows directly from knowledge of the action's nature, regardless of other considerations. Therefore, the determining factor for moral value is the essence of the act. Since this cause exists wherever the act exists, it must apply to God as well. The second proof: Reason judges the evil of injustice and falsehood from any agent, and this judgment is even stronger in the case of God because He has no excuses (ignorance or need) that might mitigate the act. Thus the judgment in the seen world (al-shahid) applies equally to the unseen (al-gha'ib). This concludes the arguments of those who claim these judgments are known necessarily.

الصدق
الكذب
الظلم
المقتضى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moral values depend on external factors like consequences or God's arbitrary command.
Correction: The proof shows that goodness is known by recognizing the act itself, not by external considerations, so it is intrinsic.

Checkpoint

A) Because it benefits people.
B) Because it is truth.
C) Because God commands it.
D) Because it is beautiful.

Key Takeaway
Goodness and evil are intrinsic to actions, so they apply to all agents, including God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever try to justify a small lie by its good consequences, even though you know deep down that falsehood is intrinsically wrong? If the cause of evil is the act itself, how can you reconcile that with your own behavior?
?
If truth is good from God and falsehood evil from Him, then when you speak falsehood, are you not acting against the very nature of the One you claim to worship? What would it take for you to be as truthful as God is truthful?
?
The passage says that whoever knows truth as truth knows it is good. You know that lying is wrong, yet you still do it. What does that say about your heart? Are you willing to commit to total honesty, even when it costs you, starting today?

The Second Proof: Reason's Stronger Judgment of God
Second proof: rational judgment of evil is even stronger for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational proof that reason judges evil from God more strongly than from humans because God has no excuses.

Core Idea
Human excuses weaken moral judgment; God's perfection strengthens it.

The second proof builds on the first. Reason judges injustice and falsehood as evil, and this judgment is even stronger when applied to God because humans may have excuses like ignorance or need, but God has none. Thus, the moral categories apply to God with greater force.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the argument that reason's judgment is universal but stronger for God.
Notice the key phrase 'أكد في العقول' (more confirmed in intellects).
2
Understand the analogy and contrast
Reason judges evil from any agent, but the presence of excuses in humans reduces the force of judgment; God's lack of excuses strengthens it.
Humans may have ignorance or need as excuses; God is free from these.
3
Apply the principle of shahid and gha'ib
The argument concludes that the same judgment that holds in the witnessed world (human actions) applies to the unseen (God's actions).
This is a common method in kalam: using analogy from the witnessed to the unseen.
4
Reflect on the overall conclusion
Consider how this proof reinforces the objectivity of moral values and their application to God.
The author closes by stating these judgments are known necessarily.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني : أن نقول : لما ثبت هذا المعنى ، وجب أن يبقى هذا الحسن والقبح في حق الله تعالى ( والذي يدل عليه ) وجهان : الأول : إن الصدق إنما حسن لكونه صدقاً ، وأن الكذب إنما قبح لكونه كذباً ، وأن الظلم إنما قبح لكونه ظلماً . والدليل عليه : أن من عرف كون الصدق صدقاً عرف كونه حسناً ، وإن جهل سائر الاعتبارات ، وإن لم يعرف كونه صدقاً لم يعرف كونه حسناً ، وإن عرف سائر الاعتبارات . وكذا القول في قبح الكذب ، وقبح الظلم . ولما كان العلم بالحسن والقبح دائراً مع العلم بهذه الوجوه والاعتبارات ، وجب أن يكون المقتضى للحسن والقبح هو هذه الوجوه والاعتبارات . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : وجب أن يكون الصدق حسناً من الله ، وأن يكون قبيحاً منه لما ثبت أنه أينما حصل المؤثر والموجب . وجب أن يحصل الأثر . الثاني : إن صريح العقل كما يحكم بقبح الظلم والكذب من الواحد منا ، فكذلك يحكم بقبحهما من الله تعالى ، بل نقول : إن قبحهما من الله تعالى أكـد في العقول لأن الواحد منا قد يظلم ويكذب، إما لجهله أو لحاجته . والجهل والحاجة عذر في ذلك الفعل من بعض الوجوه . أما الله تعالى فإنه منزه عن الجهل والعجز والحاجة ، فكان حكم العقل بقبح الكذب والظلم منه أكد وأقوى من حكمه بقبحه لهما من العبد . فيثبت أن هذا الحكم كما هو ثابت في الشاهد ثابت في الغائب . فهذا جملة الكلام في تقرير كلام القائلين بأن هذه الأحكام معلومة بالضرورة
The second station: we say: Since this meaning is established, it is necessary that this goodness and evil apply to God Almighty. That which indicates it is two aspects: The first: Verily, truth is good only because it is truth, and falsehood is evil only because it is falsehood, and injustice is evil only because it is injustice. The proof of it: that whoever knows that truth is truth knows it is good, even if he is ignorant of all other considerations; and if he does not know it is truth, he does not know it is good, even if he knows all other considerations. And likewise the statement concerning the evil of falsehood and the evil of injustice. And since knowledge of good and evil revolves with knowledge of these aspects and considerations, it must be that the cause of goodness and evil is these aspects and considerations. And if this is established, we say: It must be that truth is good from God, and that falsehood is evil from Him, because since it is established that wherever the effective cause exists, the effect must exist. The second: Verily, clear reason just as it judges the evil of injustice and falsehood from one of us, so likewise it judges their evil from God Almighty. Rather, we say: Their evil from God Almighty is more confirmed in intellects because one of us may commit injustice and falsehood either due to ignorance or need. Ignorance and need are an excuse for that action in some respects. As for God Almighty, He is transcendent above ignorance, incapacity, and need. Thus reason's judgment of the evil of falsehood and injustice from Him is more confirmed and stronger than its judgment of their evil from the servant. So it is established that this judgment, just as it is established in the witnessed (al-shahid), is established in the absent (al-gha'ib). This is the sum of speech in presenting the words of those who say that these judgments are known by necessity.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a second station (maqam) argument that the moral categories of good and evil apply to God. The author gives two proofs. The first proof: The goodness of truth and evil of falsehood and injustice are intrinsic to the actions themselves, not dependent on external factors. The proof is that knowledge of goodness follows directly from knowledge of the action's nature, regardless of other considerations. Therefore, the determining factor for moral value is the essence of the act. Since this cause exists wherever the act exists, it must apply to God as well. The second proof: Reason judges the evil of injustice and falsehood from any agent, and this judgment is even stronger in the case of God because He has no excuses (ignorance or need) that might mitigate the act. Thus the judgment in the seen world (al-shahid) applies equally to the unseen (al-gha'ib). This concludes the arguments of those who claim these judgments are known necessarily.

Key Terms
العقل
الشاهد
الغائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because God is omnipotent, He is beyond moral judgment and can do whatever He wills without moral consideration.
Correction: The proof shows that reason's judgment of evil is universal and even stronger for God due to His perfection, so moral categories apply to Him.

Checkpoint
Why is reason's judgment of evil from God stronger than from humans?
A) God is more powerful.
B) Humans have excuses like ignorance or need; God does not.
C) Reason is different for God.
D) God's actions are unknown.

Key Takeaway
Reason's moral judgment applies to all, and more strongly to God because He lacks excuses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often excuse your own sins by saying 'I didn't know' or 'I was weak.' But if you believe God has no such excuses, how does that make you feel about those sins? Will you stop excusing yourself and instead repent sincerely, knowing that the same moral standard applies to you that applies to God—except He never falls short?
?
When life is hard and you feel God's decree is unfair, do you ever nurse a secret thought that God is being unjust? This lesson tells you that reason declares injustice evil from anyone, and God above all cannot be unjust. Will you now actively fight that thought and submit to His perfect justice, even when you don't understand?
?
The proof shows that reason's judgment of evil is stronger for God because He lacks excuses. Today, when you are about to commit a wrong action, ask yourself: 'Am I acting as if I have an excuse that God doesn't have?' Then stop, and choose the path that honors the One whose judgment is absolutely confirmed.

The First Rational Proof for Moral Distinctions
First argument for rational proof of moral judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument that moral good and evil are known by rational proof, and why arbitrary divine commands are impossible.

Core Idea
Moral distinctions cannot be arbitrary; they require intrinsic grounds.

In this passage, the author presents the first proof from those who argue that moral judgments require rational proof rather than mere revelation. The argument begins with the observed division of actions into good and evil, and shows that without intrinsic grounds, any moral distinction by revelation would be arbitrary and impossible for God. It then uses a dilemma to force the conclusion that actions have inherent aspects that make them good or evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Start by reading the full Arabic passage and its literal translation. Get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice the repetition of key terms like 'حسنة' and 'قبيحة'.
2
Identify the Main Argument
The argument begins with the observation that actions are divided into good and evil. If there were no intrinsic grounds for this division, the differentiation would be a preference without a cause, which impossible for a wise Creator.
This part establishes the need for rational grounds.
3
Understand the Objection and Dilemma
An opponent might say that revelation (sharʿ) designates good and evil. The proof responds with a dilemma: either the actions designated are equal in all respects or not. If equal, the designation is arbitrary (impossible). If not equal, there must be inherent aspects that make them worthy of the designation.
This is the core logical move.
4
Reflect on the Conclusion
The proof concludes that good and evil are based on aspects inherent in the actions themselves, not merely on divine command. This is what is meant by saying 'good is good due to its own aspects.'
Think about why this is important for rational knowledge of ethics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تقرير كلام القائلين بأنا نثبت هذه الأحكام بالدليل . فهو من جهات الحجة الأولى : قالوا : رأينا الأفعال منقسمة إلى حسنة وقبيحة ، فلولا أن من الأمور ما لأجله صار البعض حسناً والبعض قبيحاً . وإلا لكان حصول هذا التفاوت ترجيحاً لاحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر من غير مرجح ، وأنه يلزم منه نفي الصانع ، وليس لقائل أن يقول : هذا التفاوت إنما حصل لان الشرع خص البعض بالحسن ، والباقي بالقبح ، لأنا نقول : الذي خصه الشرع بالحسن ، والذي خصه بالقبح. إما أن يقال : إنهما استويا في جميع والاعتبارات العائدة إليهما أو إلى أحدهما ، أو ليس الأمر كذلك ؟ والأول باطل ، وإلا لكان تخصيص الشرع بعضها بالحسن وبعضها بالقبح ترجيح طرفي الجائز على الآخر لا المرجح وأنه محال . والثاني : يفيد القول بأن كل واحد منهما قد اختص بوجه لأجله استحق أن يخصه الشرع بالحسن أو بالقبح ، ولا معنى لقولنا : إن الحسن إنما يحسن لوجوه عائدة إليه ، وأن القبح إنما يقبح لوجوه عائدة إليه ، إلا هذا المعنى
As for presenting the words of those who say that we establish these judgments by proof, it is from several aspects. The first proof: They said: We see actions divided into good and evil. Were it not that there are matters on account of which some become good and others evil, otherwise this differentiation would be a preference of one of two possible alternatives over the other without a preferring factor, and that would necessitate the negation of the Maker. And no one can say: This differentiation only occurred because the revealed law (al-sharʿ) singled out some as good and the rest as evil. For we say: That which the revealed law singled out as good, and that which it singled out as evil: either it is said that they are equal in all considerations pertaining to them or to one of them, or it is not so? The first is false, for otherwise the revealed law's singling out some as good and some as evil would be a preference of one of two possible alternatives over the other without a preferring factor, and that is impossible. The second yields the statement that each one of them is distinguished by an aspect on account of which it deserves that the revealed law singles it out as good or evil. And there is no meaning to our statement that 'the good is only good on account of aspects pertaining to it, and the evil is only evil on account of aspects pertaining to it' except this meaning.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage introduces the position of those who argue that moral judgments are known by rational proof (bi-l-dalil) rather than necessity. The author presents the first proof (al-hujjah al-ula). The argument begins from the observed division of actions into good and evil. If there were no intrinsic grounds for this division, then the differentiation would be arbitrary, a preference without a reason, which would imply the impossibility of the Creator (since such arbitrariness is impossible for a wise agent). The opponent might object that revealed law (al-sharʿ) itself designates some acts as good and others as evil. The proof responds with a dilemma: either the actions designated by law are equal in all relevant respects, or they are not. If they are equal, then the law's designation would be arbitrary (impossible). If they are not equal, then there must be some aspect inherent in the actions that makes them deserve their moral valuation. This inherent aspect is precisely what rational proof identifies, and this meaning is what is meant by saying that good is good due to aspects pertaining to itself.

Key Terms
الحجة الأولى
الدليل
الحسن والقبح
الشرع
الترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moral values are created solely by divine command without any reason or basis in the actions themselves.
Correction: This argument shows that if moral values were purely arbitrary, divine commands would be arbitrary and irrational, which is impossible for God. Therefore, actions must have intrinsic aspects that make them good or evil, and reason can know these aspects.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the first proof against those who say good and evil are only from revelation?
A) Revelation cannot change moral values.
B) Without intrinsic grounds, revelation's distinction would be arbitrary and impossible.
C) Reason is unnecessary.
D) The proof proves revelation is false.

Key Takeaway
Actions have inherent aspects that make them good or evil, and these can be known by rational proof, not solely by revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as if God's commands are arbitrary, picking and choosing which to follow based on convenience? What would it mean for you today to accept that every command of God has a rational wisdom? Name one command you currently obey reluctantly, and ask God to show you its intrinsic goodness.
?
This argument claims that moral distinctions are not just divine decrees but are rooted in reality. When you face a moral dilemma, do you first look for the rational ground, or do you rely on habit or feeling? Can you recall a time when you ignored a clear moral truth because it was inconvenient? What would change if you truly believed that good and evil are intrinsic and not just cultural or personal preferences?
?
If God's actions are never arbitrary, then every trial, blessing, or command has a purpose. Is there an area of your life where you suspect God of acting arbitrarily? How does that suspicion affect your trust in Him? Today, commit to searching for the wisdom behind one difficult aspect of your life, using the same kind of reasoning shown in this lesson.
