The Necessity Argument for Moral Knowledge: Applying Judgments to God
Summarizes the necessity argument for moral knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the necessity argument for moral knowledge applies even more strongly to God than to humans, based on the absence of excuses.

Core Idea
God's perfection makes moral judgments apply more strongly, not less.

This passage summarizes a key argument from those who hold that moral judgments like 'justice is good' and 'injustice is bad' are known by necessity. The argument extends these judgments from human actions to divine actions, arguing that God's perfection strengthens the rational conviction of their ugliness. Let's see how the author builds this case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Notice the author's concise summary style.
The passage is a conclusion; it assumes prior arguments.
2
Identify the key premise about human excuses
The author states that humans may be unjust or lie due to ignorance or need, which serve as partial excuses. Why does this matter for the argument?
3
Trace the analogy from shahid to gha'ib
The argument moves from the witnessed realm (human actions) to the absent realm (divine actions). See how the absence of excuses in God makes the judgment of ugliness even stronger.
This is a standard kalam analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكذلك يحكم بقبحهما من الله تعالى ، بل نقول : إن قبحهما من الله تعالى أكد في العقول لأن الواحد منا قد يظلم ويكذب، إما لجهله أو لحاجته . والجهل والحاجة عذر في ذلك الفعل من بعض الوجوه . أما الله تعالى فإنه منزه عن الجهل والعجز والحاجة ، فكان حكم العقل بقبح الكذب والظلم منه أكد وأقوى من حكمه بقبحه لهما من العبد . فيثبت أن هذا الحكم كما هو ثابت في الشاهد ثابت في الغائب . فهذا جملة الكلام في تقرير كلام القائلين بأن هذه الأحكام معلومة بالضرورة .
Thus He judges the ugliness of both from God Almighty. Rather, we say: their ugliness from God Almighty is stronger in the intellects because any one of us may be unjust and lie, either due to his ignorance or his need. Ignorance and need are an excuse in that action from some aspects. As for God Almighty, He is free from ignorance, incapacity, and need. So the judgment of the intellect regarding the ugliness of lying and injustice from Him is stronger and more powerful than its judgment regarding their ugliness from the servant. Thus it is established that this judgment, just as it is established in the witnessed (al-shahid), is established in the absent (al-gha'ib). This is the summary of the statement about the speech of those who say that these judgments are known by necessity (al-darurah).
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage recaps the position of those who claim that moral judgments (al-husn wa al-qubh) are known by necessity, not by reason or revelation alone. It begins by asserting that the ugliness of injustice and lying from God is even more evident in the intellect than from humans. This is because human wrongdoing often stems from ignorance or need, which serve as partial excuses. God, being free from all imperfections, has no such excuses, so the rational judgment of ugliness applies more strongly to Him. The argument then generalizes: just as these judgments apply in the witnessed realm (al-shahid, i.e., human affairs), they apply in the absent realm (al-gha'ib, i.e., divine affairs). The passage concludes by identifying this as the gist of the necessity-based view.

Key Terms
حسن وقبح (husn wa qubh)
ضرورة (darurah)
شاهد وغائب (shahid wa gha'ib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since God is beyond human categories, moral judgments do not apply to Him.
Correction: The argument shows that moral judgments apply even more strongly to God because He lacks the excuses that mitigate human wrongdoing.

Checkpoint

A) Because God is more powerful than humans.
B) Because humans have excuses like ignorance and need, whereas God has none.
C) Because God's actions are not subject to moral judgment.
D) Because the intellect is weaker when judging divine actions.

Key Takeaway
Moral judgments are known by necessity and apply to God with greater force because He is free from the excuses that mitigate human wrongdoing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If moral judgments are known by necessity, then you are held accountable not just by revelation but by your own innate reason. Are you living consistently with what you already know to be right? What specific area of your life have you been rationalizing?

First Proof: Division of Actions into Good and Evil Implies an Inherent Basis
Presents the first proof for rational moral judgment: the division of actions into good and evil requires an inherent basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the division of actions into good and evil requires an inherent basis, and how the argument refutes the objection that it is based solely on revelation.

Core Idea
Good and evil are inherent in actions, not just labels from revelation.

This lesson presents the first proof for rational moral judgment. The argument begins from the observed division of actions into good and evil and shows that this division cannot be arbitrary or purely conventional; it must be based on real aspects in the actions themselves. We'll follow the logical steps and see how the author refutes a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style and logical structure.
2
Identify the main claim and proof structure
The passage presents a proof: actions are observed to be good or evil. If no inherent basis, then the division is arbitrary, leading to impossibility. Then an objection is raised and refuted.
The proof is called al-hujja al-ula (the first proof).
3
Analyze the dilemma against the objection
The objection says the division is from revelation. The reply uses a dilemma: either the actions are equal in all aspects or not. If equal, the revelation's specification would be arbitrary; if not equal, each action has an inherent aspect that justifies its judgment.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that good and evil are inherent in actions, not merely imposed by revelation. Ask yourself: why does this matter for theology and ethics?
This connects to the broader debate on rational vs. revealed ethics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تقرير كلام القائلين بأننا نثبت هذه الأحكام بالدليل . فهو من الوجوه
الحجة الأولى : قالوا : رأينا الأفعال منقسمة إلى حسنة وقبيحة ، فلولا أن من الأمور ما لأجله صار البعض حسناً والبعض قبيحاً . وإلا لكان حصول هذا التفاوت ترجيحاً لاحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر من غير مرجح ، وأنه يلزم منه نفي الصانع ، وليس لقائل أن يقول : هذا التفاوت إنما حصل لان الشرع خص البعض بالحسن ، والباقي بالقبح ، لأنا نقول : الذي خصه الشرع بالحسن ، والذي خصه بالقبح. إما أن يقال : إنهما استويا في جميع والاعتبارات العائدة إليهما أو إلى أحدهما ، أو ليس الأمر كذلك ؟ والأول باطل ، وإلا لكان تخصيص الشرع بعضها بالحسن وبعضها بالقبح ترجيح طرفي الجائز على الآخر لا المرجح وأنه محال . والثاني : يفيد القول بأن كل واحد منهما قد اختص بوجه لأجله استحق أن يخصه الشرع بالحسن أو بالقبح ، ولا معنى لقولنا : إن الحسن إنما يحسن لوجوه عائدة إليه ، وأن القبح إنما يقبح لوجوه عائدة إليه ، إلا هذا المعنى .
As for the exposition of the statement of those who say: we establish these judgments by proof, it is from the aspects. The first proof: They said: We see actions divided into good and evil. If not for the fact that there are matters for which some become good and others evil, then the occurrence of this differentiation would be a preference of one of the two possible alternatives over the other without a preferrer, and that would entail the negation of the Creator. And no one can say: this differentiation only occurred because the revealed law (al-shar') specified some as good and the rest as evil. For we say: That which the law specified as good, and that which it specified as evil — either it is said that they are equal in all the attributes pertaining to them or to one of them, or it is not so? The first is false, otherwise the law's specification of some as good and some as evil would be a preference of one of the two possible alternatives over the other without a preferrer, and that is impossible. The second yields the statement that each one of them has been distinguished by an aspect for which it deserved that the law specify it as good or evil. And there is no meaning to our saying: 'The good is good only for aspects pertaining to it, and the evil is evil only for aspects pertaining to it' except this meaning.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage begins the section of those who argue that moral judgments are known by proof (bil-dalil). It presents the first proof (al-hujja al-ula). The argument starts from the observed fact that actions are divided into good and evil. If there were no inherent reasons why some actions are good and others evil, then this division would be arbitrary, which would be a preference without a preferrer, leading to the denial of the Creator (nafy al-sani'). The text then refutes a possible objection: that the division is due to the revealed law (al-shar'). The refutation uses a dilemma: either the actions considered by the law are equal in all respects, or they are not. If they are equal, then the law's specification would be arbitrary and impossible. If they are not equal, then each action has a specific aspect that makes it deserving of the law's judgment, and that is exactly what it means for an action to be good or evil in itself. Thus, the proof concludes that good and evil are inherent in actions, not merely conventional.

Key Terms
حسن وقبيح
شرع
ترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the law (shar') creates good and evil, making them purely conventional.
Correction: The argument shows that the law's judgments must be based on pre-existing aspects in the actions themselves; otherwise the division would be arbitrary.

Checkpoint
In the first proof, what is the consequence if the division of actions into good and evil were purely from revelation without any inherent basis?
A) It would show that God is arbitrary.
B) It would imply a preference without a preferrer, which is impossible.
C) It would mean that actions are inherently good or evil.
D) It would confirm the necessity of moral judgments.

Key Takeaway
The rational division of actions into good and evil must be based on inherent aspects in the actions themselves, not merely on divine command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if good and evil are real and objective, or do you sometimes act as if they are just opinions or cultural norms? When you make a moral decision today, pause and ask: am I treating this as an inherent truth or as a mere convention?
?
This proof argues that without inherent goodness and evil, the world would be arbitrary and meaningless. Have you ever felt tempted to dismiss moral absolutes as relative? How does this rational argument challenge that feeling, and what would it mean for your life if you truly embraced that right and wrong are woven into the fabric of reality?
?
If the law's specifications are based on real aspects in actions, then every divine command carries deep wisdom. Think of a command you struggle with. Can you search for the inherent 'aspect' that makes it good or evil, and commit to respecting that wisdom even when you don't fully understand it?
